Section 1: Indian Polity & Constitution (200 Questions)

Topics: Preamble, Fundamental Rights, DPSPs, Union & State Executive, Parliament, Judiciary, Constitutional Bodies, Amendments, Emergency Provisions, Local Self Government, Election Commission

1.1 Preamble & Basic Structure

Q.1. The word 'Socialist' and 'Secular' were added to the Preamble by which Amendment? Ans: 42nd Amendment, 1976
Explanation: Originally the Preamble said "Sovereign Democratic Republic." The 42nd Amendment (during Emergency, PM Indira Gandhi) added "Socialist" (govt will reduce inequality) and "Secular" (no state religion, equal respect for all religions).
Trick: 42nd Amendment = "Mini Constitution" — it changed the most things. Remember: 42 added Socialist+Secular to Preamble. "42 SS" (like a ship name).
Q.2. The Preamble of the Indian Constitution is based on which resolution? Ans: Objectives Resolution moved by Jawaharlal Nehru on December 13, 1946
Explanation: Before writing the Constitution, Nehru presented a vision document called "Objectives Resolution" in the Constituent Assembly. It outlined what kind of nation India should be. The Preamble is essentially a refined version of this resolution.
Trick: "Nehru's dream for India" = Objectives Resolution → became the Preamble. Date: 13 Dec 1946 (lucky 13!).
Q.3. The concept of 'Basic Structure' of the Constitution was established in which case? Ans: Kesavananda Bharati vs State of Kerala (1973)
Explanation: This landmark case decided that Parliament can amend any part of the Constitution BUT cannot destroy its "basic structure" (federalism, secularism, democracy, judicial review, etc.). This limits Parliament's amending power.
Trick: "Kesavananda = Cannot destroy basics." The case was about a Kerala temple's land. 13-judge bench (largest ever). 7:6 verdict. Remember: 1973 = Basic Structure.
Q.4. The Indian Constitution came into effect on: Ans: January 26, 1950
Explanation: This date was chosen because Jan 26, 1930 was when Congress declared "Purna Swaraj" (complete independence). We celebrate this as Republic Day. India became a Republic (elected head of state) on this day.
Trick: Adopted: Nov 26, 1949 (Constitution Day). Effective: Jan 26, 1950 (Republic Day). 2-month gap for preparation. "November adopt, January apply."
Q.5. The Constitution was adopted by the Constituent Assembly on: Ans: November 26, 1949
Explanation: After 2 years, 11 months, and 18 days of work, the Constituent Assembly adopted the Constitution. This day is now celebrated as "Constitution Day" (Samvidhan Divas) since 2015.
Trick: Nov 26 = Constitution Day (adopted). Jan 26 = Republic Day (came into effect). Both are 26th! "26-26 rule."
Q.6. Who is known as the 'Father of Indian Constitution'? Ans: Dr. B.R. Ambedkar (Chairman of Drafting Committee)
Explanation: Ambedkar chaired the 7-member Drafting Committee that actually wrote the Constitution. He was a legal scholar, economist, and champion of Dalit rights. He studied at Columbia University and London School of Economics.
Trick: Ambedkar = Father of Constitution = Chairman of Drafting Committee. Rajendra Prasad = President of Constituent Assembly (different role!). Don't confuse the two.
Q.7. The Preamble declares India as: Ans: Sovereign, Socialist, Secular, Democratic Republic
Explanation: Each word has meaning: Sovereign=no external control, Socialist=reduce inequality, Secular=no state religion, Democratic=people's rule, Republic=elected head (not king).
Trick: "SSSDR" — remember as "Super Smart Students Do Research." Or just: "So She Sat Down & Read" (Sovereign, Socialist, Secular, Democratic, Republic).
Q.8. The idea of Preamble was borrowed from which country's constitution? Ans: United States of America
Explanation: The US Constitution begins with "We the People..." — India's Preamble also starts with "We, the people of India..." The concept of a preamble (introductory statement of purpose) came from the US.
Trick: India borrowed from many countries: Preamble=USA, DPSP=Ireland, Fundamental Rights=USA, Federal structure=Canada, Emergency=Germany, Amendment procedure=South Africa. Make a table!
Q.9. Is the Preamble a part of the Constitution? Ans: Yes (as per Kesavananda Bharati case, 1973), but it is non-justiciable
Explanation: Earlier (Berubari case, 1960) SC said Preamble is NOT part. Later (Kesavananda, 1973) SC reversed this — Preamble IS part of Constitution but cannot be enforced in court (non-justiciable). It can be amended under Article 368.
Trick: "Berubari said No, Kesavananda said Yes." Preamble = part of Constitution but not enforceable. It's like a "mission statement" — guides interpretation but can't be sued upon.
Q.10. The word 'Republic' in the Preamble indicates: Ans: Head of State is elected (not hereditary)
Explanation: Republic means the highest position (President) is filled by election, not by birth/inheritance. Unlike UK (monarchy — King/Queen by birth), India's President is elected by an electoral college.
Trick: Republic = "Res Publica" (Latin) = "public thing" = people choose their leader. Opposite of Monarchy. India = Republic. UK = Constitutional Monarchy.

1.2 Fundamental Rights (Part III, Articles 12-35)

Q.11. How many Fundamental Rights are currently available in the Indian Constitution? Ans: 6 (Right to Property was removed by 44th Amendment, 1978)
Explanation: Originally 7 FRs. Right to Property (Art.31) was removed and made a legal right (Art.300A) by 44th Amendment. Current 6: (1) Equality (2) Freedom (3) Against Exploitation (4) Religion (5) Cultural & Educational (6) Constitutional Remedies.
Trick: "EF-ER-CR" = Equality, Freedom, Exploitation, Religion, Cultural, Constitutional Remedies. Or: "Every Friday Evening, Ravi Calls Chandu."
Q.12. Right to Equality is guaranteed under which Articles? Ans: Articles 14 to 18
Explanation: Art.14=Equality before law. Art.15=No discrimination. Art.16=Equal opportunity in jobs. Art.17=Abolition of untouchability. Art.18=Abolition of titles.
Trick: 14-18 = Equality (5 articles). Remember: "14 starts Equality, 19 starts Freedom." Each right spans a few articles.
Q.13. Article 14 provides: Ans: Equality before law AND equal protection of laws
Explanation: Two concepts: (1) "Equality before law" (British concept) = no one is above law. (2) "Equal protection of laws" (American concept) = similar treatment for similar people. Together they mean: law applies equally, but reasonable classification is allowed.
Trick: Art.14 has TWO parts: "Before law" (negative — no special privilege) + "Protection of laws" (positive — equal treatment). Like two sides of same coin.
Q.14. Article 15 prohibits discrimination on grounds of: Ans: Religion, race, caste, sex, or place of birth
Explanation: The state cannot discriminate based on these 5 grounds. But Art.15(3) allows special provisions for women/children, and Art.15(4) for backward classes (reservations).
Trick: 5 grounds: "RRCSP" = Religion, Race, Caste, Sex, Place of birth. Remember: "Ravi's Red Car Stopped at Petrol-pump." Note: "place of birth" not "place of residence" — this is a common exam trap!
Q.15. Article 16 deals with: Ans: Equality of opportunity in public employment
Explanation: No citizen can be denied government job based on religion, race, caste, sex, descent, place of birth, or residence. But allows reservations for backward classes (Art.16(4)).
Trick: Art.16 = Jobs equality. Art.15 = General equality. Art.16 is specifically about government employment. Private sector is NOT covered by Art.16.
Q.16. Untouchability is abolished under which Article? Ans: Article 17
Explanation: Art.17 completely abolishes untouchability and makes its practice a punishable offense. The Protection of Civil Rights Act, 1955 provides penalties. This is an ABSOLUTE right — no exceptions.
Trick: Art.17 = "17 = Untouchability GONE." It's one of the few absolute rights with no exceptions or reasonable restrictions.
Q.17. Article 19 guarantees how many freedoms? Ans: 6 freedoms (originally 7; freedom of property removed by 44th Amendment)
Explanation: The 6 freedoms: (1) Speech & expression (2) Assemble peacefully (3) Form associations/unions (4) Move freely in India (5) Reside anywhere in India (6) Practice any profession/trade. All have "reasonable restrictions."
Trick: "SAAMP" = Speech, Assembly, Association, Movement, Residence (settle), Profession. Or think: "I can SAY anything, MEET anyone, JOIN any group, GO anywhere, LIVE anywhere, DO any job."
Q.18. Right against Exploitation is covered under which Articles? Ans: Articles 23 and 24
Explanation: Art.23 = Prohibits human trafficking, begar (forced labor), and similar forms of forced labor. Art.24 = No child below 14 years can work in factories, mines, or hazardous employment.
Trick: "23-24 = Exploitation." Art.23 = forced labor (adults). Art.24 = child labor (children under 14). Remember: "23 adults, 24 children" (24 hours in a day, children need all 24 hours for growth!).
Q.19. Article 21 provides: Ans: Protection of life and personal liberty
Explanation: "No person shall be deprived of his life or personal liberty except according to procedure established by law." This is the MOST important article — SC has expanded it to include right to livelihood, privacy, dignity, clean environment, health, education, shelter, speedy trial, and more.
Trick: Art.21 = "Life + Liberty" = the SUPER article. Almost everything has been read into Art.21. Even right to sleep, right to reputation! Cannot be suspended even during Emergency.
Q.20. Right to Education (Article 21A) was added by which Amendment? Ans: 86th Amendment, 2002
Explanation: Made free and compulsory education for children aged 6-14 a Fundamental Right. The RTE Act, 2009 was passed to implement this. It also added a Fundamental Duty for parents (Art.51A(k)).
Trick: "86th = Education." RTE Act came 7 years later in 2009. Age group: 6-14 (8 years of schooling). Below 6 = not covered (DPSP under Art.45).
Q.21. Article 21A provides free and compulsory education for children of age group: Ans: 6 to 14 years
Explanation: Only children between 6 and 14 years have this as a Fundamental Right. Below 6 years (early childhood care) is covered under DPSP Art.45, not as a FR.
Trick: "6 to 14" = Class 1 to Class 8 approximately. 8 years of free education. Remember: "6-14 = FR, below 6 = DPSP."
Q.22. Which Article empowers the Supreme Court to issue writs for enforcement of Fundamental Rights? Ans: Article 32
Explanation: Art.32 gives citizens the right to directly approach the Supreme Court if their Fundamental Rights are violated. SC can issue 5 types of writs. This right itself is a Fundamental Right!
Trick: "32 = SC writs for FR only." "226 = HC writs for FR + other rights." SC scope is narrow (only FR), HC scope is wider (any legal right). Art.32 = "Heart and Soul" of Constitution (Ambedkar).
Q.23. Which Article empowers High Courts to issue writs? Ans: Article 226
Explanation: HC can issue writs for enforcement of Fundamental Rights AND for "any other purpose" (any legal right). This makes HC's writ jurisdiction WIDER than SC's. But SC's writs are guaranteed as FR (can't be suspended).
Trick: "226 = HC, wider power." "32 = SC, narrower but guaranteed." If your FR is violated → go to SC (Art.32) or HC (Art.226). If other legal right violated → only HC (Art.226).
Q.24. Dr. Ambedkar called which Article as the 'Heart and Soul' of the Constitution? Ans: Article 32 (Right to Constitutional Remedies)
Explanation: Without Art.32, all other Fundamental Rights would be meaningless — you'd have rights on paper but no way to enforce them. Art.32 gives you the REMEDY (writs) to enforce your rights. That's why it's the "heart and soul."
Trick: "Rights without remedies = useless." Art.32 = the remedy article = heart & soul. It's like having a warranty card — without it, your product guarantee means nothing.
Q.25. The five writs that can be issued are: Ans: Habeas Corpus, Mandamus, Prohibition, Certiorari, Quo Warranto
What are Writs? Writs are orders issued by courts to protect citizens' rights. Think of them as "court commands" to authorities.

All 5 Writs Explained:
1. Habeas Corpus ("produce the body") — If someone is illegally detained/arrested, court orders: "Bring this person before me." Protects personal liberty.
2. Mandamus ("we command") — Court orders a public official: "Do your duty!" Used when govt officer refuses to act. Cannot be issued against President/Governor or private persons.
3. Prohibition ("to forbid") — Higher court tells lower court: "Stop! Don't proceed with this case, you don't have jurisdiction." Only court-to-court.
4. Certiorari ("to be certified") — Higher court tells lower court: "Send me your case records for review." Used to quash wrong orders. Only court-to-court.
5. Quo Warranto ("by what authority?") — Court asks a person: "Show me your right to hold this public office." Challenges illegal appointments.

Memory Trick (HMPCQ): "Habeas Mandamus Prohibition Certiorari Quo-warranto" = "Have My Passport Checked Quickly"

Key Differences:
• SC issues writs ONLY for Fundamental Rights (Art.32)
• HC issues writs for Fundamental Rights AND other legal rights (Art.226) — wider scope
• Habeas Corpus can be issued against private persons too (only writ that can!)
Q.26. Which writ is issued to produce a detained person before the court? Ans: Habeas Corpus
Explanation: Latin meaning: "You shall have the body." If police arrests someone illegally, or a person is kidnapped, the court issues Habeas Corpus to produce that person. It's the most powerful writ for personal freedom.
Trick: "Habeas = Have the body" — imagine a judge saying "HAVE this person brought to me!" It's about physical freedom/detention.
Q.27. Which writ is issued to a public authority to perform its duty? Ans: Mandamus
Explanation: Latin meaning: "We command." If a government officer refuses to issue your passport, or a university won't give your degree — court issues Mandamus ordering them to do their job. Cannot be issued against: President, Governor, private persons, or for discretionary duties.
Trick: "Mandamus = Man, DO IT!" — court commanding an officer to perform their mandatory duty. Think of a boss ordering an employee.
Q.28. Fundamental Rights can be suspended during: Ans: National Emergency (Article 352), except Articles 20 and 21
Explanation: During National Emergency, the President can suspend all FRs under Art.19 automatically. Other FRs can be suspended by Presidential order under Art.359. BUT Articles 20 (protection against double jeopardy/self-incrimination) and 21 (life & liberty) can NEVER be suspended — even during Emergency.
Trick: "20 and 21 are UNTOUCHABLE" — they survive even Emergency. Remember: 44th Amendment (1978) made Art.20 & 21 non-suspendable (earlier only Art.20 was protected). "20-21 = Always alive."
Q.29. Article 25-28 deal with: Ans: Right to Freedom of Religion
Explanation: Art.25 = Freedom of conscience, practice & propagate religion. Art.26 = Freedom to manage religious affairs. Art.27 = No tax for promotion of any religion. Art.28 = No religious instruction in state-funded schools.
Trick: "25-28 = Religion (4 articles)." India is secular = state has no religion, but citizens can follow any religion. Key: Art.25 says "propagate" (spread) but NOT "convert" — this is an exam favorite!
Q.30. Cultural and Educational Rights are covered under: Ans: Articles 29 and 30
Explanation: Art.29 = Protection of interests of minorities (any section with distinct language, script, or culture). Art.30 = Right of minorities to establish and administer educational institutions. These protect minority identity.
Trick: "29-30 = Minorities' education & culture." Art.30 is why minority institutions (like AMU, Jamia, Christian colleges) have special autonomy. Only RELIGIOUS and LINGUISTIC minorities are covered.

1.3 Directive Principles of State Policy (Part IV, Articles 36-51)

Q.31. DPSPs are borrowed from which country's constitution? Ans: Ireland
Explanation: Ireland's Constitution has "Directive Principles of Social Policy" — India borrowed this concept. DPSPs are guidelines for the government to create a welfare state. They're like a "to-do list" for the government.
Trick: India borrowed from many countries. Key ones: DPSP=Ireland, FR=USA, Federal=Canada, Emergency=Germany, Concurrent List=Australia, Republic=France, Amendment=South Africa. Make a chart!
Q.32. DPSPs are: Ans: Non-justiciable (cannot be enforced by courts)
Explanation: Unlike Fundamental Rights, you cannot go to court if the government doesn't follow DPSPs. They are "moral obligations" — the government SHOULD follow them but isn't legally forced to. However, they are "fundamental in governance."
Trick: FR = Justiciable (court can enforce) = Individual rights. DPSP = Non-justiciable (court can't enforce) = Government duties. Think: "FR = your rights, DPSP = govt's homework."
Q.33. Article 39A (Equal Justice and Free Legal Aid) was added by which Amendment? Ans: 42nd Amendment, 1976
Explanation: Art.39A directs the state to provide free legal aid to poor citizens so that justice is not denied due to poverty. This was added by the 42nd Amendment (the same one that added Socialist/Secular to Preamble).
Trick: 42nd Amendment added MANY things: Socialist+Secular to Preamble, Fundamental Duties, Art.39A, Art.48A (environment). Remember "42 = the big one."
Q.34. Article 40 deals with: Ans: Organization of Village Panchayats
Explanation: Art.40 directs the state to organize village panchayats and give them powers for self-government. This was later implemented through the 73rd Amendment (1992) which added Part IX to the Constitution.
Trick: "Art.40 = Panchayats (DPSP)." "73rd Amendment = Panchayats (actual implementation)." "74th Amendment = Municipalities." Remember: 73=rural, 74=urban.
Q.35. Article 44 provides for: Ans: Uniform Civil Code for all citizens
Explanation: UCC means one common set of personal laws (marriage, divorce, inheritance, adoption) for ALL citizens regardless of religion. Currently, different religions follow different personal laws. This is one of the most debated DPSPs.
Trick: "Art.44 = UCC = One law for all." Currently only Goa has a form of UCC. It's in DPSP (not FR), so government can't be forced to implement it. Highly debated topic in exams.
Q.36. Article 48 deals with: Ans: Prohibition of cow slaughter and organization of agriculture
Explanation: Art.48 directs the state to organize agriculture and animal husbandry on modern lines, and to prohibit slaughter of cows, calves, and other milch/draught cattle. Many states have enacted cow protection laws based on this.
Trick: "Art.48 = Cows & Agriculture." Art.48A (added by 42nd Amendment) = Environment protection. Don't confuse 48 and 48A!
Q.37. Article 48A (Protection of Environment) was added by: Ans: 42nd Amendment, 1976
Explanation: Art.48A directs the state to protect and improve the environment and safeguard forests and wildlife. This was added along with Art.51A(g) which makes it a Fundamental Duty of citizens too.
Trick: Environment is both DPSP (Art.48A — govt's duty) AND Fundamental Duty (Art.51A(g) — citizen's duty). Both added by 42nd Amendment. "Environment = double duty."
Q.38. Article 51 deals with: Ans: Promotion of international peace and security
Explanation: Art.51 directs India to: (a) promote international peace, (b) maintain just relations between nations, (c) respect international law, (d) encourage settlement of disputes by arbitration. This is why India supports UN, NAM, etc.
Trick: "Art.51 = International peace." Art.51A = Fundamental Duties (different!). Don't confuse Art.51 (DPSP about world peace) with Art.51A (citizens' duties). The 'A' makes all the difference.
Q.39. Fundamental Duties were added by which Amendment? Ans: 42nd Amendment, 1976 (Part IVA, Article 51A)
Explanation: Based on Swaran Singh Committee recommendations. Originally 10 duties were added. They are moral obligations of citizens (like respecting flag, protecting environment, developing scientific temper). They are non-justiciable like DPSPs.
Trick: "42nd Amendment = Duties added." Based on USSR (Soviet Union) model. Part IVA, Art.51A. "Swaran Singh Committee recommended them." Non-justiciable = can't be enforced by court.
Q.40. How many Fundamental Duties are there currently? Ans: 11 (originally 10; 11th added by 86th Amendment, 2002)
Explanation: 10 duties added by 42nd Amendment (1976). 11th duty added by 86th Amendment (2002): "Duty of parents/guardians to provide education to children aged 6-14." This was added along with Right to Education (Art.21A).
Trick: "10 original + 1 education = 11 total." The 11th duty about education was added same time as RTE (86th Amendment). Key duties to remember: respect Constitution/flag/anthem, protect environment, develop scientific temper, safeguard public property.

1.4 Union Executive

Q.41. The President of India is elected by: Ans: Electoral College (elected members of both Houses of Parliament + elected members of State Legislative Assemblies + Delhi & Puducherry)
Explanation: The President is NOT directly elected by people. An Electoral College elects them through proportional representation with single transferable vote. Nominated members and State Legislative Council members are NOT part of this college.
Trick: "Elected members only" — nominated members excluded. Electoral College = Lok Sabha + Rajya Sabha (elected only) + State Assemblies (elected only) + Delhi & Puducherry assemblies. NO MLCs, NO nominated members.
Q.42. The minimum age to become President of India is: Ans: 35 years
Explanation: Qualifications: (1) Indian citizen (2) Age 35+ (3) Qualified to be Lok Sabha member (4) Not hold any office of profit.
Trick: Age limits: President/VP/Governor = 35. Rajya Sabha = 30. Lok Sabha/MLA = 25. Panchayat = 21. Remember: "35-30-25-21" (decreasing by 5 then 4).
Q.43. The President's term of office is: Ans: 5 years
Explanation: 5 years from the date of assuming office. Can be re-elected any number of times (no limit like USA's 2-term limit). Can resign by writing to Vice-President.
Trick: Almost everything in Indian polity is 5 years: President, VP, Governor, Lok Sabha, State Assembly, Panchayat, Municipality. Exception: Rajya Sabha member = 6 years.
Q.44. The President can be removed by: Ans: Impeachment (Article 61) — by Parliament with 2/3rd majority in each House
Explanation: Grounds: "Violation of the Constitution." Process: Either House initiates with 14 days notice signed by 1/4th members. That House passes with 2/3rd majority. Other House investigates and passes with 2/3rd majority. No Indian President has ever been impeached.
Trick: "Impeachment = 2/3rd in BOTH houses." Only for "violation of Constitution" (not misbehavior like judges). Process takes minimum 14 days. Remember: President=impeachment, Judge=removal (different terms, similar process).
Q.45. Who administers oath to the President? Ans: Chief Justice of India
Explanation: CJI administers oath to President. If CJI is unavailable, the senior-most judge of SC does it. The President then administers oath to the Vice-President, PM, Governors, etc.
Trick: "CJI swears in President. President swears in everyone else." Chain: CJI → President → VP/PM/Governors/Ministers. Governor swears in CM and state ministers.
Q.46. The President's power to grant pardon is under: Ans: Article 72
Explanation: President can grant: (1) Pardon — completely absolves (2) Commutation — changes punishment type (death→life) (3) Remission — reduces sentence duration (4) Respite — lesser punishment due to special reason (5) Reprieve — temporary stay of execution.
Trick: 5 types of clemency: "PCRRR" = Pardon, Commutation, Remission, Respite, Reprieve. President's power covers: court martial cases, Union law offenses, death sentences. Governor (Art.161) has similar power but CANNOT pardon death sentences or court martial cases.
Q.47. The real executive power in India is exercised by: Ans: Prime Minister and Council of Ministers (Article 74)
Explanation: India has a Parliamentary system where President is nominal/ceremonial head and PM is the real executive. President acts on PM's advice (42nd Amendment made this binding, 44th said President can ask to reconsider once but must accept if sent again).
Trick: "President = rubber stamp (mostly)." Real power = PM + Cabinet. India = Parliamentary system (like UK). USA = Presidential system (President has real power). This is a fundamental difference asked in exams.
Q.48. Article 74 states that: Ans: There shall be a Council of Ministers with PM at the head to aid and advise the President
Explanation: This article makes it MANDATORY for the President to have a Council of Ministers. The President MUST act on their advice (44th Amendment: can return once for reconsideration, but must accept if sent back unchanged).
Trick: "Art.74 = PM advises President (binding)." "Art.75 = PM appointed by President, Ministers appointed on PM's advice." 74 and 75 together define the PM-President relationship.
Q.49. The Vice-President is the ex-officio Chairman of: Ans: Rajya Sabha
Explanation: VP's primary role is chairing Rajya Sabha sessions. When VP acts as President (if President dies/resigns/is removed), they CANNOT chair Rajya Sabha during that period. Deputy Chairman takes over.
Trick: "VP = RS Chairman." "Speaker = LS Chairman." VP gets salary as RS Chairman (not as VP!). VP has no specific constitutional functions except: (1) Chair RS (2) Act as President when vacancy arises.
Q.50. The Vice-President is elected by: Ans: Members of both Houses of Parliament (Electoral College)
Explanation: VP's electoral college = ALL members (elected + nominated) of BOTH Houses of Parliament. This is different from President's election (which includes state assemblies and excludes nominated members).
Trick: "President = elected + state assemblies." "VP = all members of Parliament only (no states)." VP election includes nominated members; President election excludes them. This difference is a common exam question!

1.5 Parliament

Q.51. The Parliament of India consists of: Ans: President + Rajya Sabha + Lok Sabha
Explanation: Parliament is NOT just the two Houses. It's President + RS + LS (Art.79). President is part of Parliament — that's why bills need Presidential assent to become law. Without President's signature, no law is complete.
Trick: "Parliament = President + 2 Houses." Many people forget the President is part of Parliament. UK: Parliament = Monarch + House of Lords + House of Commons (same concept).
Q.52. Maximum strength of Lok Sabha is: Ans: 552 (530 from states + 20 from UTs + 2 Anglo-Indians nominated — Anglo-Indian nomination discontinued by 104th Amendment, 2020)
Explanation: Current effective maximum = 550 (530+20). The 2 Anglo-Indian seats were abolished in 2020. Present actual strength = 543 (530 states + 13 UTs). Members directly elected by people.
Trick: "LS = 552 max (now effectively 550)." "RS = 250 max." LS members are directly elected (first-past-the-post). RS members are indirectly elected by state MLAs (proportional representation).
Q.53. Maximum strength of Rajya Sabha is: Ans: 250 (238 elected by state/UT assemblies + 12 nominated by President)
Explanation: 238 members elected by state legislative assemblies through proportional representation. 12 nominated by President from people with expertise in literature, science, art, social service. Current actual strength = 245.
Trick: "RS = 238 + 12 = 250." The 12 nominated members are experts (not politicians). They can vote on all matters EXCEPT President's election. Remember: "12 wise people nominated to RS."
Q.54. Minimum age for Lok Sabha membership is: Ans: 25 years
Trick: Age ladder: LS/MLA = 25, RS/MLC = 30, President/VP/Governor = 35. "25-30-35" — increases by 5 for each higher body. Voter age = 18 (reduced from 21 by 61st Amendment, 1989).
Q.55. Minimum age for Rajya Sabha membership is: Ans: 30 years
Explanation: RS is the "Council of Elders" — hence higher age requirement (30 vs 25 for LS). The idea is that RS members should be more experienced and mature.
Trick: "RS = upper house = older members = 30 years." "LS = lower house = younger allowed = 25 years." Upper house everywhere has higher age: US Senate=30, House=25 (same pattern!).
Q.56. Money Bill can be introduced only in: Ans: Lok Sabha (Article 109)
Explanation: Money Bills deal with taxation, government spending, borrowing, etc. Only LS can introduce them (with President's prior recommendation). RS can only suggest amendments within 14 days — LS can accept or reject suggestions. RS cannot reject a Money Bill.
Trick: "Money = Lok Sabha's power." RS is powerless on Money Bills — can only delay by 14 days. Speaker of LS certifies whether a bill is a Money Bill (final authority, cannot be questioned). This makes LS more powerful than RS on financial matters.
Q.57. Joint sitting of Parliament is called under: Ans: Article 108 (presided by Speaker of Lok Sabha)
Explanation: Joint sitting is called when there's a deadlock between the two Houses on an ORDINARY bill (not Money Bill or Constitutional Amendment Bill). President calls it. Speaker presides. Decision by majority of total members present and voting.
Trick: "Joint sitting = deadlock breaker for ordinary bills ONLY." NOT for: Money Bills (LS dominates anyway) or Constitutional Amendment Bills (each house must pass separately). Only 3 joint sittings have happened: 1961 (Dowry), 1978 (Banking), 2002 (POTA).
Q.58. Rajya Sabha is a: Ans: Permanent body (not subject to dissolution; 1/3rd members retire every 2 years)
Explanation: RS never dissolves — it's continuous. Every 2 years, 1/3rd of members retire and new ones are elected. Each member serves 6 years. This ensures institutional memory and continuity even when LS is dissolved.
Trick: "RS = permanent, never dies." "LS = can be dissolved." RS member term = 6 years (longest of any elected body in India). 1/3rd retire every 2 years (6÷3=2). This is why RS is called "permanent house."
Q.59. The Speaker of Lok Sabha is elected by: Ans: Members of Lok Sabha from among themselves
Explanation: Speaker is elected by LS members after a general election. Usually from the ruling party. Speaker is expected to be impartial. Can vote only in case of a tie (casting vote). Resigns by writing to Deputy Speaker.
Trick: "Speaker = elected by LS, from LS." Speaker doesn't vote normally (only casting vote in tie). Speaker remains in office even after LS dissolution — until new LS elects new Speaker. Pro-tem Speaker (senior-most member) presides until then.
Q.60. No-confidence motion can be moved only in: Ans: Lok Sabha
Explanation: The Council of Ministers is collectively responsible to Lok Sabha (Art.75). If LS passes a no-confidence motion, the entire government must resign. RS cannot move no-confidence motion because government is not responsible to RS.
Trick: "Government responsible to LS only." No-confidence = LS. This is why PM must be LS member (or become one within 6 months). RS member can be PM but must win LS seat within 6 months (like Manmohan Singh initially entered via RS).
Q.61. The quorum for a meeting of Parliament is: Ans: 1/10th of total members of the House
Explanation: Quorum = minimum members needed to conduct business. For LS: 1/10th of 545 ≈ 55 members. For RS: 1/10th of 245 ≈ 25 members. If quorum not met, Speaker/Chairman adjourns the House.
Trick: "1/10th = quorum for Parliament." For state legislatures also 1/10th (but minimum 10). This is surprisingly low — Parliament can technically function with just 55 members present!
Q.62. A bill becomes an Act after: Ans: President's assent
Explanation: Bill's journey: Introduced in one House → Passed by that House → Sent to other House → Passed by other House → Sent to President → President gives assent → Becomes Act/Law. President can: (1) Give assent (2) Withhold assent (3) Return for reconsideration (not Money Bills).
Trick: "Bill + President's signature = Act." Without assent, it remains a bill forever. President can use "pocket veto" (neither sign nor return — just keep it pending indefinitely). Zail Singh did this with Indian Post Office Amendment Bill.
Q.63. The term of Lok Sabha is: Ans: 5 years (can be extended during National Emergency by 1 year at a time)
Explanation: Normal term = 5 years from first meeting. Can be dissolved earlier by President on PM's advice. During Emergency, can be extended by Parliament by 1 year at a time (no limit on extensions during Emergency). After Emergency ends, cannot extend beyond 6 months.
Trick: "LS = 5 years, extendable in Emergency." This happened during 1975 Emergency — 5th Lok Sabha was extended to 6 years. RS members = 6 years (fixed, no extension).
Q.64. Rajya Sabha has special power under Article 249 to: Ans: Authorize Parliament to make laws on State List subjects in national interest
Explanation: If RS passes a resolution by 2/3rd majority that it's "necessary in national interest," Parliament can make laws on State List subjects for up to 1 year (renewable). This is RS's unique power that LS doesn't have.
Trick: "Art.249 = RS's special power over State List." Also Art.312: RS can create All-India Services by 2/3rd resolution. These two are RS's exclusive powers. Remember: "249 and 312 = RS special."
Q.65. Zero Hour in Parliament starts at: Ans: 12 noon (it is an Indian innovation, not mentioned in rules of procedure)
Explanation: Zero Hour is the time immediately after Question Hour (which ends at 12 noon). Members raise urgent matters without prior notice. It's called "Zero" because it starts at 12:00 (zero minutes past noon). It's NOT in the rules — it's a convention.
Trick: "Zero Hour = 12 noon = Indian innovation." Question Hour = first hour (11 AM to 12 noon). Zero Hour = after 12 noon. Neither is in the Constitution — both are procedural conventions. Zero Hour is unique to Indian Parliament.

1.6 Judiciary

Q.66. The Supreme Court of India was established under: Ans: Article 124
Explanation: Art.124 establishes the SC and provides for appointment of judges. SC started functioning on Jan 28, 1950 (2 days after Republic Day) with 8 judges. Now has 34 judges (1 CJI + 33 others).
Trick: "124 = SC established." "214 = HC established." Remember: SC articles start from 124, HC articles from 214. Difference of 90.
Q.67. The Chief Justice of India is appointed by: Ans: President of India (based on Collegium recommendation)
Explanation: Convention: senior-most judge of SC becomes CJI. The Collegium (CJI + 4 senior judges) recommends appointments. President formally appoints. This system was established by the Three Judges Cases (1993, 1998).
Trick: "Collegium system = judges appoint judges." Not in Constitution — evolved through judicial interpretation. NJAC (National Judicial Appointments Commission) was struck down in 2015 as unconstitutional.
Q.68. Judges of Supreme Court retire at the age of: Ans: 65 years
Trick: "SC = 65, HC = 62." Remember: Supreme Court is HIGHER, so retirement age is also HIGHER (65 > 62). 3-year difference. This is one of the most frequently asked factual questions.
Q.69. Judges of High Court retire at the age of: Ans: 62 years
Explanation: HC judges retire at 62 (originally 60, raised to 62 by 15th Amendment, 1963). After retirement, SC judges cannot practice in any court. HC judges cannot practice in the same HC (but can in SC or other HCs).
Trick: "62 for HC, 65 for SC." Post-retirement: SC judge → can't practice anywhere. HC judge → can't practice in same HC. This restriction ensures judicial independence.
Q.70. Supreme Court's original jurisdiction includes: Ans: Disputes between Centre and States, or between States (Article 131)
Explanation: Original jurisdiction = cases that START directly in SC (not appeals). Only federal disputes: Centre vs State, State vs State, Centre+State vs State. Individual citizens cannot use Art.131.
Trick: SC has 3 jurisdictions: (1) Original (Art.131) = federal disputes. (2) Appellate (Art.132-136) = appeals from HC. (3) Advisory (Art.143) = President asks SC's opinion. "OAA = Original, Appellate, Advisory."
Q.71. Judicial Review power of Supreme Court is under: Ans: Articles 13, 32, and 136
Explanation: Judicial Review = power to declare laws/executive actions unconstitutional. Art.13: laws violating FR are void. Art.32: SC can issue writs. Art.136: Special Leave Petition (appeal against any court/tribunal). This makes judiciary the "guardian of Constitution."
Trick: "Judicial Review = checking if laws follow Constitution." India borrowed this from USA. It's part of Basic Structure (can't be removed by amendment). SC is the final interpreter of Constitution.
Q.72. Public Interest Litigation (PIL) can be filed under: Ans: Article 32 (in SC) and Article 226 (in HC)
Explanation: PIL allows any public-spirited person to file a case on behalf of those who can't (poor, illiterate, disadvantaged). You don't need to be personally affected. Even a letter/postcard to the court can be treated as PIL.
Trick: "PIL = anyone can file for public good." Started in 1980s. No court fee needed. Can be filed by letter. Expanded access to justice for common people. Also called "Social Action Litigation."
Q.73. The concept of PIL was introduced by: Ans: Justice P.N. Bhagwati and Justice V.R. Krishna Iyer
Explanation: These two judges revolutionized Indian judiciary in the 1980s by allowing any person to approach courts for public interest matters. First PIL: Hussainara Khatoon case (1979) — about undertrial prisoners languishing in Bihar jails.
Trick: "Bhagwati + Krishna Iyer = PIL pioneers." They made courts accessible to the poor. Remember: PIL is NOT in the Constitution — it's a judicial innovation (like Zero Hour in Parliament).
Q.74. A judge of Supreme Court can be removed by: Ans: Impeachment by Parliament (on grounds of proved misbehavior or incapacity)
Explanation: Process: Motion signed by 100 LS members or 50 RS members → Investigation committee → If proved → Each House passes with special majority (2/3rd of present + majority of total). President then removes. No SC/HC judge has ever been removed in India.
Trick: "Judges removed for: Misbehavior or Incapacity (MI)." "President removed for: Violation of Constitution." Different grounds! Process is similar (Parliament, special majority) but grounds differ. Justice Ramaswami case (1993) — motion failed in LS.
Q.75. Article 141 states that: Ans: Law declared by Supreme Court is binding on all courts in India
Explanation: SC's decisions are the "law of the land." All High Courts and lower courts MUST follow SC's interpretation. This ensures uniformity in law across India. SC can overrule its own previous decisions.
Trick: "Art.141 = SC law is supreme for all courts." HC decisions bind lower courts in that state only. SC decisions bind ALL courts everywhere. SC can overrule itself; HC cannot overrule SC.

1.7 Constitutional Bodies

Q.76. Election Commission of India is established under: Ans: Article 324
Explanation: EC supervises elections to Parliament, State Legislatures, President, and VP. It's an independent constitutional body. Currently has 1 CEC + 2 Election Commissioners. CEC can only be removed like SC judge (impeachment).
Trick: "324 = Election Commission." EC is independent — not under any ministry. CEC has same protection as SC judge (removal only by impeachment). Other ECs can be removed by President on CEC's recommendation.
Q.77. The Chief Election Commissioner can be removed by: Ans: Same procedure as removal of Supreme Court Judge (impeachment by Parliament)
Explanation: CEC has security of tenure — can only be removed by Parliament through impeachment (proved misbehavior/incapacity, special majority in both Houses). This ensures EC's independence from government pressure.
Trick: "CEC removal = same as SC judge." But other Election Commissioners can be removed by President on CEC's advice (lesser protection). This asymmetry is important for exams.
Q.78. Comptroller and Auditor General (CAG) is appointed under: Ans: Article 148
Explanation: CAG audits all government accounts (Centre + States). Appointed by President. Serves for 6 years or until age 65 (whichever is earlier). Called "Guardian of Public Purse." Removal same as SC judge.
Trick: "148 = CAG." "CAG = Guardian of Public Purse." CAG audits → reports to President/Governor → laid before Parliament/Legislature → examined by Public Accounts Committee (PAC). CAG is the backbone of financial accountability.
Q.79. CAG submits audit reports to: Ans: President (for Union accounts) / Governor (for State accounts)
Explanation: CAG doesn't submit to Parliament directly. Reports go to President → President lays them before Parliament. For states: CAG → Governor → State Legislature. Then PAC (Public Accounts Committee) examines these reports.
Trick: "CAG → President → Parliament → PAC examines." The chain is important. CAG finds problems, PAC investigates them. PAC is chaired by opposition leader (convention). This ensures checks and balances on government spending.
Q.80. Union Public Service Commission (UPSC) is constituted under: Ans: Article 315
Explanation: UPSC conducts exams for All-India Services (IAS, IPS, IFS) and Central Services. It's a constitutional body — independent of government. Chairman and members appointed by President, serve 6 years or until age 65.
Trick: "315 = UPSC." "320 = Functions of UPSC." UPSC is advisory — government can ignore its advice (but must report to Parliament why). SPSC (State PSC) = Art.315 too (same article covers both).
Q.81. Finance Commission is constituted under: Ans: Article 280
Explanation: FC recommends how to distribute tax revenue between Centre and States. It's a quasi-judicial body appointed by President every 5 years. Current: 16th FC (chaired by Arvind Panagariya, 2024-29).
Trick: "280 = Finance Commission." FC decides "who gets how much money" — Centre vs States. It's like a family budget mediator. Key recommendation: what % of central taxes go to states (currently ~41%).
Q.82. The Finance Commission is appointed every: Ans: 5 years
Explanation: President constitutes a new FC every 5 years (or earlier if needed). Each FC has a chairman + 4 members. They study Centre-State financial relations and recommend tax-sharing formula.
Trick: "FC = every 5 years." 15th FC (2020-25) recommended 41% of central taxes to states. 16th FC (2024-29) is the latest. FC recommendations are advisory — government usually accepts them.
Q.83. National Commission for SCs is under: Ans: Article 338
Explanation: Investigates complaints of SC community, monitors safeguards, advises on socio-economic development. Has powers of a civil court. Reports to President annually.
Trick: "338 = SC Commission." "338A = ST Commission." "338B = OBC Commission (added by 102nd Amendment, 2018)." Remember: 338, 338A, 338B — alphabetical order matches SC, ST, OBC.
Q.84. National Commission for STs is under: Ans: Article 338A
Explanation: Separated from SC Commission by 89th Amendment (2003). Earlier both SC and ST were under one commission (Art.338). Now separate commissions for focused attention on tribal issues.
Trick: "89th Amendment split SC and ST commissions." Before 2003: one combined commission. After: separate Art.338 (SC) and Art.338A (ST). ST commission focuses on tribal land rights, forest rights, displacement issues.
Q.85. Attorney General of India is appointed under: Ans: Article 76
Explanation: AG is the highest law officer of India. Appointed by President. Must be qualified to be SC judge. Can participate in Parliament proceedings but cannot vote. Not a government servant — can take private cases too.
Trick: "76 = AG (Attorney General)." AG advises government on legal matters. Advocate General (Art.165) is the state equivalent. AG can appear in any court in India. Salary decided by President (no fixed amount in Constitution).

1.8 Emergency Provisions

Q.86. National Emergency is declared under: Ans: Article 352 (on grounds of war, external aggression, or armed rebellion)
Explanation: President declares on written advice of Cabinet (not just PM). Must be approved by Parliament within 1 month by special majority. Can continue indefinitely with Parliament's approval every 6 months. Affects entire country or part of it.
Trick: "352 = National Emergency." Three grounds: War, External Aggression, Armed Rebellion (originally "internal disturbance" — changed to "armed rebellion" by 44th Amendment). Remember: "352-356-360" = three types of emergency in sequence.
Q.87. President's Rule in a State is imposed under: Ans: Article 356 (failure of constitutional machinery in a state)
Explanation: When state government can't function per Constitution, President takes over state administration. Governor reports to President. State Assembly is dissolved or suspended. Parliament makes laws for that state. Most misused provision in Indian history.
Trick: "356 = President's Rule = State Emergency." S.R. Bommai case (1994) limited its misuse — SC said it's subject to judicial review. Used 100+ times! Most in: Punjab, UP, Kerala. Rarest: states with stable governments.
Q.88. Financial Emergency is declared under: Ans: Article 360
Explanation: Declared when financial stability/credit of India is threatened. President can reduce salaries of all government servants including judges. Never been declared in India's history — not even once!
Trick: "360 = Financial Emergency = NEVER USED." It's the nuclear option for finances. Can reduce salaries, direct states on money matters. The fact that it's never been used (even during 1991 crisis) shows how extreme it is.
Q.89. National Emergency has been declared how many times in India? Ans: 3 times
Explanation: (1) 1962 — Chinese aggression (lasted till 1968). (2) 1971 — Indo-Pak war (Bangladesh liberation). (3) 1975 — "Internal disturbance" by Indira Gandhi (the controversial Emergency, lasted till 1977). The 3rd one led to 44th Amendment reforms.
Trick: "3 Emergencies: 62, 71, 75." First two = external wars (justified). Third = internal (controversial, led to reforms). 1975 Emergency is the most asked — it led to 42nd (during) and 44th (after) Amendments.
Q.90. During National Emergency, which Fundamental Rights cannot be suspended? Ans: Article 20 (Protection against conviction) and Article 21 (Right to Life)
Explanation: 44th Amendment (1978) made Art.20 and 21 non-suspendable even during Emergency. Art.20: no double jeopardy, no self-incrimination, no ex-post-facto law. Art.21: right to life and liberty. These are absolute even in worst times.
Trick: "20 and 21 = IMMORTAL rights." Even if India is at war, you can't be killed without due process (Art.21) or punished twice for same crime (Art.20). Before 44th Amendment, only Art.20 was protected. 44th added Art.21 too.
Q.91. The maximum period of President's Rule without Parliament's approval is: Ans: 2 months
Explanation: President's Rule must be approved by Parliament within 2 months. If not approved, it lapses. After approval, continues for 6 months. Can be extended every 6 months up to maximum 3 years.
Trick: "2 months without approval, then 6-month extensions, max 3 years." Timeline: 0-2 months (automatic), 2-6 months (first approval), then every 6 months (re-approval needed). After 1 year, extension needs special conditions (Election Commission certifies elections can't be held).
Q.92. The maximum period of President's Rule is: Ans: 3 years (with Parliament's approval every 6 months)
Explanation: After 1 year, extension requires: (1) National Emergency in force in that state, OR (2) Election Commission certifies elections cannot be held. Maximum = 3 years total. After that, elections MUST be held.
Trick: "Max 3 years." But in practice, most President's Rule lasts a few months until new elections. 3 years is the absolute constitutional limit. Punjab had longest President's Rule (1987-1992 = 5 years, but through special legislation).
Q.93. The 44th Amendment (1978) replaced 'internal disturbance' with: Ans: 'Armed rebellion' as ground for National Emergency
Explanation: After 1975 Emergency (declared on vague ground of "internal disturbance"), the 44th Amendment tightened the language. Now Emergency can only be declared for "armed rebellion" — a much higher threshold than mere "disturbance."
Trick: "44th Amendment = anti-Emergency reforms." It undid many 42nd Amendment changes. Key changes: internal disturbance→armed rebellion, written Cabinet advice needed, Art.21 made non-suspendable, Right to Property removed from FRs.

1.9 Important Amendments

Q.94. 1st Amendment (1951) dealt with: Ans: Added 9th Schedule, restrictions on freedom of speech, land reform laws
Explanation: First Amendment was needed because courts were striking down land reform laws as violating Right to Property. So government added 9th Schedule (laws here can't be challenged in court) and added "reasonable restrictions" to Art.19.
Trick: "1st Amendment = land reforms + 9th Schedule." It was passed within 1 year of Constitution! Shows how quickly practical problems arose. 9th Schedule = "safe vault" for laws government wants to protect from courts.
Q.95. 7th Amendment (1956) dealt with: Ans: Reorganization of States on linguistic basis
Explanation: After States Reorganisation Commission (Fazl Ali Commission) recommended reorganizing states by language, this amendment redrew India's map. Created 14 states and 6 UTs. Andhra Pradesh was first linguistic state (1953, before this amendment).
Trick: "7th Amendment = linguistic states (1956)." Fazl Ali Commission recommended it. First linguistic state = Andhra Pradesh (1953, carved from Madras). Potti Sriramulu's fast unto death triggered the movement.
Q.96. 42nd Amendment (1976) is known as: Ans: 'Mini Constitution' (most comprehensive amendment ever)
Explanation: Passed during Emergency by Indira Gandhi's government. Added: Socialist+Secular to Preamble, Fundamental Duties, DPSP supremacy over FR, reduced judicial power, extended Parliament's term. Most controversial amendment in history.
Trick: "42nd = Mini Constitution = Emergency era." It changed SO many things that it's called a mini constitution. Based on Swaran Singh Committee. Many of its provisions were later reversed by 43rd and 44th Amendments.
Q.97. 44th Amendment (1978) dealt with: Ans: Reversed many changes of 42nd Amendment, removed Right to Property from FRs
Explanation: Passed by Janata Party government (post-Emergency). Key changes: Right to Property removed from FR (made legal right under Art.300A), "internal disturbance"→"armed rebellion," Art.20+21 made non-suspendable, restored judicial powers.
Trick: "44th = anti-42nd = post-Emergency corrections." Think of 42nd and 44th as opposites: 42nd gave more power to government, 44th took it back. 44th is the "democracy restoration" amendment.
Q.98. 73rd Amendment (1992) deals with: Ans: Panchayati Raj Institutions (Part IX added to Constitution)
Explanation: Gave constitutional status to Panchayats (village self-government). Created 3-tier system: Gram Panchayat (village), Panchayat Samiti (block), Zilla Parishad (district). 1/3rd reservation for women. 5-year term. State Election Commission conducts elections.
Trick: "73rd = Panchayats (rural)." "74th = Municipalities (urban)." Both in 1992. Remember: 73=rural, 74=urban. 11th Schedule (29 subjects for Panchayats). Came into effect April 24, 1993 (Panchayati Raj Day).
Q.99. 74th Amendment (1992) deals with: Ans: Municipalities/Urban Local Bodies (Part IXA added)
Explanation: Constitutional status to urban local bodies. Three types: Nagar Panchayat (transitional area), Municipal Council (smaller urban), Municipal Corporation (larger urban). 1/3rd women reservation. 12th Schedule (18 subjects).
Trick: "74th = Municipalities = Urban." Three types: NP < MC < Corporation (increasing size). 12th Schedule = 18 subjects for municipalities. Came into effect June 1, 1993.
Q.100. 101st Amendment (2016) deals with: Ans: Goods and Services Tax (GST)
Explanation: Introduced GST — "One Nation, One Tax." Replaced multiple indirect taxes (excise, VAT, service tax, etc.) with one unified tax. Created GST Council (Art.279A) with Centre + all states. Implemented July 1, 2017.
Trick: "101st = GST (2016)." GST Council decides rates. 5 slabs: 0%, 5%, 12%, 18%, 28%. Art.279A = GST Council. GST replaced 17 taxes and 13 cesses. "One Nation One Tax" is the slogan.

1.10 Union-State Relations & Federalism

Q.101. The 7th Schedule of the Constitution contains: Ans: Three lists — Union List (98 subjects), State List (59 subjects), Concurrent List (52 subjects)
Explanation: Union List: Defence, Foreign Affairs, Banking, Railways (only Centre can make laws). State List: Police, Public Health, Agriculture (only State). Concurrent List: Education, Forests, Marriage (both can make laws; if conflict, Centre prevails).
Trick: "7th Schedule = 3 Lists." Union=defence/money/foreign. State=police/land/health. Concurrent=education/forests/criminal law. If in doubt about which list: "Does it cross state borders?" Yes=Union. "Is it purely local?" Yes=State. "Both?" = Concurrent.
Q.102. Residuary powers belong to: Ans: Union/Central Government (Article 248)
Explanation: Any subject NOT in any of the 3 lists belongs to Centre. This is borrowed from Canada. In USA, residuary powers go to states (opposite!). This makes India's federalism "Centre-heavy."
Trick: "Residuary = Centre (Art.248)." India = Centre-heavy federation. USA/Australia = State-heavy. Example: Cyber crimes, space technology — not in any list when Constitution was written, so Centre makes laws on them.
Q.103. Inter-State Council is established under: Ans: Article 263
Explanation: A body to resolve disputes between states and between Centre-States. President establishes it. Chaired by PM. All CMs and 6 Union Ministers are members. Sarkaria Commission (1988) recommended its permanent establishment.
Trick: "263 = Inter-State Council." It's for COOPERATION between Centre and States. Different from Art.131 (SC resolves disputes judicially). ISC is political/administrative resolution; Art.131 is legal resolution.
Q.104. The Governor of a State is appointed by: Ans: President of India (Article 155)
Explanation: Governor is NOT elected — appointed by President (on Centre's advice). Serves at "pleasure of President" (can be removed anytime without reason). Acts as Centre's representative in the state. Must be from outside the state (convention, not rule).
Trick: "Governor = Centre's agent in state." Appointed by President, not elected. No fixed tenure (serves at pleasure). Can be transferred/removed anytime. This is why Governors are often controversial — seen as Centre's tool to control states.
Q.105. Minimum age to become Governor is: Ans: 35 years
Trick: Same as President and VP = 35 years. "All top constitutional posts = 35." Governor qualifications: Indian citizen, 35+ years, not member of Parliament/Legislature, not hold office of profit. Convention: should be from different state.
Q.106. The Chief Minister is appointed by: Ans: Governor (leader of majority party in State Legislative Assembly)
Explanation: Governor invites the leader of the party/coalition with majority in State Assembly to form government. CM is the real executive head of the state (like PM at Centre). CM advises Governor on minister appointments.
Trick: "Governor appoints CM" (like President appoints PM). CM must prove majority on floor of Assembly. If no clear majority → Governor uses discretion (most controversial power of Governor).
Q.107. State Legislature consists of: Ans: Governor + Legislative Assembly (+ Legislative Council in some states)
Explanation: All states have Legislative Assembly (Vidhan Sabha). Only 6 states also have Legislative Council (Vidhan Parishad). Governor is part of state legislature (like President is part of Parliament).
Trick: "State Legislature = Governor + Assembly (+ Council if exists)." Unicameral = only Assembly (most states). Bicameral = Assembly + Council (6 states). Council is like Rajya Sabha at state level.
Q.108. How many states have Legislative Council (Vidhan Parishad)? Ans: 6 states — Andhra Pradesh, Telangana, Bihar, Uttar Pradesh, Maharashtra, Karnataka
Explanation: Legislative Council is the upper house at state level. Members are partly elected (by MLAs, local bodies, graduates, teachers) and partly nominated by Governor. Term = 6 years (1/3rd retire every 2 years, like RS).
Trick: "6 states with Council: AT-BU-MK" = AP, Telangana, Bihar, UP, Maharashtra, Karnataka. Or remember: "All Tall Boys Use Many Knives." Parliament can create/abolish a Council if state assembly passes resolution by special majority.
Q.109. Zonal Councils were created by: Ans: States Reorganisation Act, 1956
Explanation: Zonal Councils are STATUTORY bodies (not constitutional) created to promote inter-state cooperation. They discuss common issues like border disputes, water sharing, transport. Home Minister chairs all Zonal Councils.
Trick: "Zonal Councils = statutory (not constitutional)." Created by Act of Parliament, not by Constitution. They're advisory — can't enforce decisions. Different from Inter-State Council (Art.263) which is constitutional.
Q.110. How many Zonal Councils are there? Ans: 5 — Northern, Southern, Eastern, Western, Central
Explanation: (1) Northern: J&K, HP, Punjab, Haryana, Rajasthan, Delhi, Chandigarh. (2) Southern: AP, Telangana, TN, Kerala, Karnataka, Puducherry. (3) Eastern: Bihar, WB, Odisha, Jharkhand, Sikkim. (4) Western: Goa, Gujarat, Maharashtra, Dadra. (5) Central: UP, MP, Chhattisgarh, Uttarakhand.
Trick: "5 Zonal Councils: N-S-E-W-C." North-East has a separate council (North Eastern Council, created by NEC Act 1971 — not a Zonal Council). So total advisory bodies = 5 Zonal + 1 NEC = 6.

1.11 Local Self Government

Q.111. 73rd Amendment provides for: Ans: Three-tier Panchayati Raj system (Gram Panchayat → Panchayat Samiti/Mandal → Zilla Parishad)
Explanation: Three levels: Village (Gram Panchayat), Block/Mandal (Panchayat Samiti), District (Zilla Parishad). Direct elections at all levels. 1/3rd seats reserved for women. SC/ST reservation proportional to population. 5-year term.
Trick: "3 tiers: Village-Block-District." Think bottom-up: Gram Panchayat (smallest) → Mandal/Block (middle) → Zilla Parishad (largest). Balwant Rai Mehta Committee (1957) first recommended 3-tier system. 73rd Amendment made it constitutional.
Q.112. Reservation for women in Panchayats is: Ans: Not less than 1/3rd (33%) of total seats
Explanation: 1/3rd of all seats AND 1/3rd of chairperson positions at all levels reserved for women. Many states have increased this to 50% (like Bihar, Rajasthan, HP, MP, Uttarakhand). This has dramatically increased women's political participation at grassroots.
Trick: "1/3rd = minimum for women in Panchayats." Some states give 50%. This is different from Parliament (Women's Reservation Bill for 33% in Parliament passed as 106th Amendment, 2023, but not yet implemented).
Q.113. State Election Commission conducts elections to: Ans: Panchayats and Municipalities (local body elections)
Explanation: SEC is different from ECI (Election Commission of India). ECI conducts Parliament and State Assembly elections. SEC conducts Panchayat and Municipality elections. SEC is appointed by Governor (Art.243K).
Trick: "ECI = Parliament + Assemblies." "SEC = Panchayats + Municipalities." Two separate bodies for two levels of elections. SEC is a state body; ECI is a central body.
Q.114. The 11th Schedule contains: Ans: 29 subjects for Panchayats
Explanation: Added by 73rd Amendment. Lists 29 subjects that states MAY devolve to Panchayats (agriculture, land improvement, minor irrigation, animal husbandry, fisheries, education, health, etc.). States decide how much power to actually give.
Trick: "11th Schedule = 29 subjects = Panchayats." "12th Schedule = 18 subjects = Municipalities." Remember: 11 comes before 12, Panchayats (rural) came before Municipalities (urban) historically. 29 > 18 because rural areas have more diverse needs.
Q.115. The 12th Schedule contains: Ans: 18 subjects for Municipalities
Explanation: Added by 74th Amendment. Lists 18 subjects for urban local bodies: urban planning, roads, water supply, public health, fire services, slum improvement, parks, burial grounds, etc.
Trick: "12th = 18 = Municipalities." Urban subjects: think of city problems — roads, water, sewage, planning, fire, parks. Rural (11th Schedule) has agriculture, irrigation, fisheries — things you won't find in cities.
Q.116. District Planning Committee is constituted under: Ans: Article 243ZD
Explanation: DPC consolidates plans prepared by Panchayats and Municipalities in the district into one integrated development plan. 4/5th members elected by Panchayat/Municipality members. Chaired by District Collector or elected member.
Trick: "243ZD = District Planning Committee." It bridges rural (Panchayat) and urban (Municipality) planning at district level. Think of it as the "coordinator" between village and city plans.
Q.117. State Finance Commission for Panchayats is under: Ans: Article 243I
Explanation: Governor appoints SFC every 5 years to recommend: (1) distribution of taxes between state and Panchayats, (2) grants-in-aid to Panchayats, (3) measures to improve Panchayat finances. Similar to Finance Commission at Centre-State level.
Trick: "243I = State Finance Commission." Just like FC (Art.280) distributes money between Centre-States, SFC distributes between State-Panchayats. Same concept, different level.
Q.118. The term of Panchayat is: Ans: 5 years
Explanation: Fixed 5-year term. Elections must be held BEFORE the term expires. If dissolved early, elections must be held within 6 months. The reconstituted Panchayat serves only the REMAINING period (not a fresh 5 years).
Trick: "Panchayat = 5 years (same as Lok Sabha)." Key difference: if dissolved early, new Panchayat only serves remaining term. This prevents deliberate dissolution for political gain.
Q.119. PESA Act (1996) extends Panchayati Raj to: Ans: Scheduled Areas (5th Schedule areas — tribal areas)
Explanation: PESA = Panchayats (Extension to Scheduled Areas) Act. Gives tribal communities self-governance through Gram Sabhas. Gram Sabha has power over natural resources, land acquisition, money lending. Protects tribal customs and traditions.
Trick: "PESA = Panchayats for Tribals." 5th Schedule areas (tribal) were excluded from 73rd Amendment. PESA fills that gap. Gram Sabha (not Gram Panchayat) is supreme in PESA areas. Key: ownership of minor forest produce given to tribals.
Q.120. 74th Amendment provides for how many types of municipalities? Ans: 3 types — Nagar Panchayat (transitional area), Municipal Council (smaller urban area), Municipal Corporation (larger urban area)
Explanation: (1) Nagar Panchayat: rural area transitioning to urban. (2) Municipal Council: small/medium towns. (3) Municipal Corporation: large cities (headed by Mayor). Governor decides which category a town falls in based on population, revenue, etc.
Trick: "3 types: NP < MC < Corporation" (increasing size/population). Nagar Panchayat = smallest urban body. Corporation = biggest (Delhi, Mumbai, Hyderabad). Mayor heads Corporation; Chairman heads Council/NP.

1.12 Schedules of the Constitution

Q.121. How many Schedules are there in the Indian Constitution? Ans: 12 Schedules
Explanation: Originally 8 Schedules. Now 12 (9th added by 1st Amendment, 10th by 52nd, 11th by 73rd, 12th by 74th Amendment). Schedules contain detailed provisions that would make the main articles too long.
Trick: "12 Schedules." Key ones to memorize: 1st=States/UTs, 3rd=Oaths, 7th=3 Lists, 8th=Languages, 9th=Protected laws, 10th=Anti-defection, 11th=Panchayat subjects, 12th=Municipality subjects.
Q.122. 1st Schedule contains: Ans: List of States and Union Territories (with their territories)
Explanation: Lists all 28 states and 8 UTs with their boundaries. Updated whenever states are created/reorganized (like Telangana added in 2014, J&K reorganized in 2019).
Trick: "1st Schedule = States & UTs." Currently: 28 states + 8 UTs = 36 entities. Latest changes: Telangana (2014), J&K split into 2 UTs (2019). This schedule changes most frequently.
Q.123. 8th Schedule contains: Ans: List of recognized/official languages (currently 22 languages)
Explanation: Originally 14 languages. Now 22 (last additions: Bodo, Dogri, Maithili, Santhali by 92nd Amendment, 2003). These languages get representation in Official Language Commission and can be used for UPSC exams.
Trick: "8th Schedule = 22 languages." Latest 4 added (2003): "BDMS" = Bodo, Dogri, Maithili, Santhali. Hindi is NOT the "national language" — it's an "official language." India has no national language! This is a very common exam trap.
Q.124. 9th Schedule was added by: Ans: 1st Amendment (1951) — contains laws that cannot be challenged in court for violating Fundamental Rights
Explanation: Laws placed in 9th Schedule are immune from judicial review (originally). But IR Coelho case (2007) said: laws added after April 24, 1973 (Kesavananda date) CAN be reviewed if they violate basic structure. Currently has 284 laws.
Trick: "9th Schedule = judicial immunity vault." Added to protect land reform laws from being struck down. But after 2007 (IR Coelho case), it's not fully immune anymore — basic structure test applies to post-1973 additions.
Q.125. 10th Schedule deals with: Ans: Anti-defection law (added by 52nd Amendment, 1985)
Explanation: Prevents elected members from switching parties after election ("floor crossing"). If a member defects, they lose their seat. Exceptions: merger of 2/3rd of party members. Speaker/Chairman decides defection cases.
Trick: "10th Schedule = Anti-defection = 52nd Amendment (1985)." Added after political instability of 1960s-80s (frequent horse-trading). Speaker decides — but this has been criticized as Speakers are often biased. 91st Amendment (2003) removed "1/3rd split" exception.
Q.126. 5th Schedule deals with: Ans: Administration of Scheduled Areas and Scheduled Tribes
Explanation: Scheduled Areas are tribal-majority regions that need special protection. Governor has special powers here — can modify/exclude central/state laws. Tribes Advisory Council advises Governor. Applies to 10 states (AP, Telangana, Jharkhand, Chhattisgarh, MP, Maharashtra, Gujarat, Rajasthan, Odisha, HP).
Trick: "5th Schedule = Tribal areas (mainland India)." "6th Schedule = Tribal areas (North-East only)." 5th = Governor has power. 6th = Autonomous District Councils have power. Remember: 5=mainland, 6=NE.
Q.127. 6th Schedule deals with: Ans: Administration of Tribal Areas in NE states (Assam, Meghalaya, Tripura, Mizoram)
Explanation: Creates Autonomous District Councils (ADCs) with legislative, judicial, and executive powers for tribal areas in 4 NE states. ADCs can make laws on land, forests, marriage, inheritance. More autonomy than 5th Schedule areas.
Trick: "6th Schedule = 4 NE states: AMTM" (Assam, Meghalaya, Tripura, Mizoram). ADCs have their own elected councils — almost like mini-states within states. They protect unique tribal customs of NE India.

1.13 Miscellaneous Polity

Q.128. Official language of the Union is: Ans: Hindi in Devanagari script (Article 343)
Explanation: Hindi is the OFFICIAL language, NOT "national language" (India has no national language!). English continues as associate official language. 22 languages in 8th Schedule are recognized. States can have their own official languages.
Trick: "Hindi = Official, NOT National." This is the #1 exam trap in language questions. Art.343 = Hindi. Art.351 = duty to promote Hindi. 8th Schedule = 22 languages. Official Languages Act, 1963 continued English indefinitely.
Q.129. Article 370 (now abrogated) dealt with: Ans: Special status to Jammu & Kashmir
Explanation: Gave J&K its own constitution, separate flag, autonomy in all matters except defence/foreign affairs/communications. Abrogated on August 5, 2019 by Presidential Order. J&K split into 2 UTs (J&K with legislature, Ladakh without). SC upheld abrogation in December 2023.
Trick: "Art.370 = J&K special status = GONE (2019)." Art.35A (special privileges for J&K residents) also abrogated simultaneously. J&K is now a UT with legislature (like Delhi/Puducherry). Ladakh is UT without legislature.
Q.130. Article 371D deals with: Ans: Special provisions for Andhra Pradesh (now applicable to Telangana too)
Explanation: Provides for equitable opportunities in education and employment for people of different regions of the state. Established Administrative Tribunal for service matters. Added by 32nd Amendment (1973) after Telangana agitation.
Trick: "371D = AP/Telangana special provisions." After bifurcation (2014), applies to both states. Ensures local candidates get preference in jobs/education in their region. AP Reorganisation Act, 2014 extended it to Telangana.
Q.131. Right to Information Act was passed in: Ans: 2005
Explanation: RTI gives citizens the right to access government information. Any citizen can file RTI application (Rs.10 fee). Government must respond within 30 days. Covers all public authorities. Exceptions: national security, personal privacy, cabinet papers.
Trick: "RTI = 2005." Key facts: Rs.10 fee, 30-day response time, Central Information Commission (CIC) is appellate authority. RTI is derived from Art.19(1)(a) — freedom of speech includes right to information. Powerful tool for transparency.
Q.132. Lokpal and Lokayuktas Act was passed in: Ans: 2013
Explanation: Lokpal = anti-corruption ombudsman at Centre. Lokayukta = at state level. Lokpal can investigate PM (with some restrictions), ministers, MPs, and Group A/B officers. First Lokpal: Justice Pinaki Chandra Ghose (2019). Anna Hazare's movement (2011) pushed this legislation.
Trick: "Lokpal Act = 2013, First Lokpal = 2019." Took 6 years to appoint! Concept first recommended by 1st Administrative Reforms Commission (1966). Took 47 years to become law! "Lok" = people, "Pal" = protector.
Q.133. National Human Rights Commission was established in: Ans: 1993 (under Protection of Human Rights Act, 1993)
Explanation: NHRC investigates human rights violations by government agencies. Chairperson must be retired CJI. Has powers of a civil court. Can recommend compensation but cannot enforce (only recommendatory). Established after international pressure on India's human rights record.
Trick: "NHRC = 1993." Chairman = retired CJI. It's a statutory body (not constitutional). Can investigate violations within 1 year of occurrence. State HRCs also exist. NHRC is like a watchdog — barks but can't bite (recommendatory only).
Q.134. The concept of 'Rule of Law' is borrowed from: Ans: England (A.V. Dicey)
Explanation: Rule of Law means: (1) No one is above law (2) Everyone is equal before law (3) Constitution is the supreme law. A.V. Dicey, a British jurist, developed this concept. It's the foundation of Art.14 (equality before law).
Trick: "Rule of Law = England = Dicey." Three principles: supremacy of law, equality before law, predominance of legal spirit. India follows this — even PM/President are subject to law (though with some immunities during office).
Q.135. The concept of 'Fundamental Rights' is borrowed from: Ans: United States of America (Bill of Rights)
Explanation: USA's Bill of Rights (first 10 amendments) inspired India's Fundamental Rights. Both protect individual liberties against state action. But India's FRs have more "reasonable restrictions" than USA's absolute rights.
Trick: India borrowed from MANY countries. Master table:
• USA: FR, Judicial Review, Preamble, Impeachment, VP as RS Chairman
• UK: Parliamentary system, Rule of Law, CAG, Writs
• Ireland: DPSP, Presidential election method
• Canada: Federation with strong Centre, Residuary powers to Centre
• Australia: Concurrent List, Joint Sitting, Freedom of trade
• Germany: Emergency provisions, Suspension of FR
• South Africa: Amendment procedure
• Japan: "Procedure established by law"
• France: Republic, Liberty-Equality-Fraternity
Q.136. The concept of 'Concurrent List' is borrowed from: Ans: Australia
Explanation: Australia's constitution has a concurrent list where both federal and state governments can make laws. India adopted this — Concurrent List has 52 subjects (education, forests, marriage, etc.) where both Centre and State can legislate.
Trick: "Concurrent = Australia." Also from Australia: Joint Sitting of Parliament, Freedom of inter-state trade (Art.301). Remember: "Australia = sharing" (concurrent means sharing power).
Q.137. The concept of 'Emergency Provisions' is borrowed from: Ans: Germany (Weimar Constitution)
Explanation: Germany's Weimar Republic (1919-1933) had emergency provisions that allowed the President to suspend rights. India borrowed this concept. Ironically, these provisions were misused in Germany (Hitler used them to become dictator) and in India (1975 Emergency).
Trick: "Emergency = Germany (Weimar)." The Weimar Constitution's emergency powers led to Hitler's rise. India's framers included them hoping they'd be used responsibly. 1975 showed the risk — leading to 44th Amendment safeguards.
Q.138. The concept of 'Amendment Procedure' is borrowed from: Ans: South Africa
Explanation: India's amendment procedure (Art.368) is neither too rigid (like USA) nor too flexible (like UK). It's a middle path — some provisions need simple majority, some need special majority, some need state ratification. This balance came from South Africa.
Trick: "Amendment = South Africa." Three types of amendments: (1) Simple majority (like ordinary law). (2) Special majority (2/3rd present + majority of total). (3) Special majority + ratification by half the states. Most amendments need type 2.
Q.139. The concept of 'Federation with strong Centre' is borrowed from: Ans: Canada
Explanation: Canada has a federal system where the central government is stronger than provinces. India adopted this — Centre can override states in many situations (Emergency, Art.249, residuary powers). India is sometimes called "quasi-federal."
Trick: "Strong Centre = Canada." India is NOT a true federation like USA (where states are very powerful). India = "Union of States" (Art.1) — deliberately avoids the word "federation." Centre can even change state boundaries without state's consent (Art.3)!
Q.140. The concept of 'Procedure established by law' is borrowed from: Ans: Japan
Explanation: Art.21 says no one can be deprived of life/liberty "except according to procedure established by law." This means any law passed by legislature is valid (even if unfair). Contrast with USA's "due process of law" (courts can check if law is fair). After Maneka Gandhi case (1978), India effectively adopted "due process" through interpretation.
Trick: "Procedure established by law = Japan." But after Maneka Gandhi case (1978), SC said the procedure must be "fair, just, and reasonable" — effectively making it "due process." So India now has BOTH concepts combined!
Q.141. Who presides over the joint sitting of Parliament? Ans: Speaker of Lok Sabha
Explanation: In joint sitting (Art.108), Speaker presides. If Speaker is absent, Deputy Speaker. If both absent, Deputy Chairman of RS. If all absent, any member chosen by members present. Decision by majority of total members present and voting.
Trick: "Joint sitting = Speaker presides." This gives LS an advantage because: (1) Speaker is from LS, (2) LS has more members (543 vs 245), so LS view usually wins in joint sitting. That's why joint sitting favors LS.
Q.142. The concept of 'Judicial Review' is borrowed from: Ans: USA
Explanation: Judicial Review = courts can declare laws unconstitutional. Started in USA with Marbury vs Madison (1803). In India, SC can strike down any law that violates the Constitution. It's part of Basic Structure — cannot be removed.
Trick: "Judicial Review = USA = Marbury vs Madison." In India: Art.13 (laws violating FR are void), Art.32/226 (writs), Art.136 (SLP). Judicial Review makes judiciary the "guardian of Constitution."
Q.143. Article 312 deals with: Ans: All India Services
Explanation: Parliament can create new All India Services if Rajya Sabha passes a resolution by 2/3rd majority. These services serve both Centre and States. Officers are recruited by UPSC but serve under state governments on deputation.
Trick: "312 = All India Services." RS has special power here (not LS). Currently 3 AIS: IAS (1946), IPS (1948), IFoS (1966). Sardar Patel called IAS the "steel frame of India."
Q.144. How many All India Services are there? Ans: 3 — IAS (Indian Administrative Service), IPS (Indian Police Service), IFoS (Indian Forest Service)
Explanation: IAS and IPS existed before independence (as ICS and IP). IFoS created in 1966. These officers are recruited by Centre (UPSC) but serve states. They can be transferred between Centre and States — ensuring national integration.
Trick: "3 AIS: IAS, IPS, IFoS." Don't confuse with Central Services (IRS, IFS-Foreign, etc.) which serve only Centre. AIS officers serve BOTH Centre and States — that's what makes them "All India."
Q.145. Citizenship provisions are in: Ans: Part II (Articles 5-11)
Explanation: Constitution provides for citizenship at commencement (1950). Parliament can make laws on acquisition/termination of citizenship later. Citizenship Act, 1955 (amended multiple times, latest CAA 2019) governs current citizenship rules.
Trick: "Part II = Citizenship (Art.5-11)." India has SINGLE citizenship (unlike USA where you're citizen of both country and state). Borrowed from UK. You're an Indian citizen — not a "Telangana citizen" or "UP citizen."
Q.146. Single citizenship concept is borrowed from: Ans: England (United Kingdom)
Explanation: In India, there's only ONE citizenship — Indian. No state citizenship. In USA, you have dual citizenship (US citizen + state citizen like "citizen of Texas"). India chose single citizenship to promote national unity.
Trick: "Single citizenship = UK = national unity." Despite being federal, India has single citizenship. This means: you can live, work, own property anywhere in India regardless of which state you're from (Art.19 — freedom of movement).
Q.147. The Constitution originally had how many Articles and Schedules? Ans: 395 Articles and 8 Schedules (in 22 Parts)
Explanation: When adopted on Nov 26, 1949: 395 Articles, 8 Schedules, 22 Parts. Now: ~470+ Articles (many added), 12 Schedules (4 added), 25 Parts. It's the longest written constitution in the world.
Trick: "Original: 395 Articles, 8 Schedules." "Now: 470+ Articles, 12 Schedules." India's Constitution is the LONGEST in the world (originally ~145,000 words). USA's is the shortest (4,543 words). Length because India is diverse and needed detailed provisions.
Q.148. Who was the President of the Constituent Assembly? Ans: Dr. Rajendra Prasad
Explanation: Rajendra Prasad presided over the Constituent Assembly sessions. He later became India's first President (1950-1962, served 2 terms — only President to do so). Don't confuse: Ambedkar = Chairman of Drafting Committee (wrote it). Rajendra Prasad = President of Assembly (presided over debates).
Trick: "Rajendra Prasad = President of CA = later 1st President of India." "Ambedkar = Drafting Committee Chairman = Father of Constitution." "Nehru = moved Objectives Resolution." Three different roles — exam loves to confuse these!
Q.149. Who was the Constitutional Advisor to the Constituent Assembly? Ans: B.N. Rau (Benegal Narsing Rau)
Explanation: B.N. Rau prepared the initial draft of the Constitution which the Drafting Committee (under Ambedkar) then refined. He studied constitutions of many countries and prepared a comprehensive draft. He later became India's representative to the UN and ICJ judge.
