With A>B, C=3rd, DExplanation: Place C on 3rd (fixed). A above B means A's floor > B's floor. D below E means E's floor > D's floor. Try: Floor 5=A, 4=E, 3=C, 2=B, 1=D. Check: A>B ✓, DTrick: Floor puzzles: (1) Fix any definite positions first. (2) Use above/below as inequalities. (3) Try arrangements and eliminate contradictions.
Q.1329. Scheduling: A works on Monday, B on Tuesday, C not on Wednesday. If D works day after A, when does D work? Ans: Tuesday
Explanation: A works Monday. D works day after A = Tuesday. (Note: B also works Tuesday — this might mean they work together, or the problem needs more constraints for unique assignment.)
Trick: "Day after X" = X's day + 1. "Day before X" = X's day - 1. Convert days to numbers (Mon=1, Tue=2...) for easier calculation.
Q.1330. Truth-teller/Liar puzzle: A says "B is a liar." B says "A and C are liars." If there is exactly one liar, who is it? Ans: B is the liar.
Explanation: Assume B is the liar: Then B's statement "A and C are liars" is FALSE → A and C are NOT both liars → at least one is truthful. A says "B is a liar" = TRUE (consistent with A being truthful). C is also truthful. Only 1 liar (B) ✓. Verify: If A were the liar, A's statement "B is liar" is false → B is truthful. B says "A and C are liars" must be true → both A and C are liars. But that's 2 liars, contradicts "exactly one." ✗.
Trick: Truth/Liar method: Assume each person is the liar one by one. Check if the assumption leads to exactly the stated number of liars without contradiction.

9.13 Verbal Reasoning

Q.1331. Statement: "All employees must attend the training." Which is a valid inference? Ans: No employee is exempt from training.
Explanation: "All must attend" = "None can skip" = "No one is exempt." These are logically equivalent statements. If ALL must do X, then NO ONE is excused from X.
Trick: "All A must B" = "No A can avoid B" = "No A is exempt from B." Convert universal positive to universal negative for inference questions.
Q.1332. Assertion: India is a democracy. Reason: People elect their representatives. Ans: Both A and R are true, and R is the correct explanation of A.
Explanation: India IS a democracy (true). People DO elect representatives (true). The act of electing representatives is what MAKES it a democracy — so R correctly explains A.
Trick: Assertion-Reason: (1) Is A true? (2) Is R true? (3) Does R explain WHY A is true? If all yes → "Both true, R explains A." If R is true but doesn't explain A → "Both true, R not explanation."
Q.1333. Course of Action: "Traffic jams are increasing in the city." Action: Build more flyovers. Ans: Follows (practical solution).
Explanation: The action directly addresses the problem (traffic jams) with a feasible solution (flyovers reduce congestion). It's practical, implementable, and relevant.
Trick: Course of action follows if: (1) It addresses the problem directly. (2) It's practically feasible. (3) It's not extreme or impractical. Actions like "ban all vehicles" = doesn't follow (too extreme).
Q.1334. Conclusion from passage: "Education empowers people. Empowered people contribute to nation building." Conclusion: Education contributes to nation building. Ans: Valid conclusion (logical chain).
Explanation: Education → Empowerment → Nation building. By transitivity: Education → Nation building. This is a valid logical deduction from the given statements.
Trick: If A leads to B, and B leads to C, then A leads to C. This transitive chain is always a valid conclusion.
Q.1335. Analogy: ABCD : ZYXW :: EFGH : ? Ans: VUTS
Explanation: A(1)→Z(26), B(2)→Y(25), C(3)→X(24), D(4)→W(23). Pattern: each letter maps to its reverse (position + reverse position = 27). E(5)→V(22), F(6)→U(21), G(7)→T(20), H(8)→S(19) = VUTS.
Trick: Reverse alphabet pairs always sum to 27: A-Z(1+26), B-Y(2+25), C-X(3+24)... Memorize: A↔Z, B↔Y, C↔X, D↔W, E↔V, F↔U, G↔T, H↔S, I↔R, J↔Q, K↔P, L↔O, M↔N.
Q.1336. If MACHINE is coded as 19-7-9-14-15-20-11, what is the code for PENCIL? Ans: P=22, E=11, N=20, C=9, I=15, L=18 → 22-11-20-9-15-18
Explanation: Check pattern: M=13, coded as 19 (13+6). A=1, coded as 7 (1+6). C=3, coded as 9 (3+6). Each letter's position +6 = code. P=16+6=22, E=5+6=11, N=14+6=20, C=3+6=9, I=9+6=15, L=12+6=18.
Trick: When code is numbers, find the shift: First letter position vs first code number. M=13, code=19, shift=+6. Verify with second letter. Apply same shift to all.
Q.1337. Odd one out: Hearing, Smelling, Tasting, Walking, Seeing Ans: Walking
Explanation: Hearing, Smelling, Tasting, Seeing are four of the five senses. Walking is a physical activity/movement, not a sense.
Trick: Five senses: Sight, Hearing, Smell, Taste, Touch. Anything not in this list is the odd one when grouped with senses.
Q.1338. Odd one out: Nile, Amazon, Sahara, Ganga Ans: Sahara
Explanation: Nile, Amazon, Ganga are rivers. Sahara is a desert (largest hot desert in the world). It doesn't belong to the "rivers" category.
Trick: Look for the category that 3 items share. Rivers are water bodies; desert is land. The geographical feature that's different is the answer.
Q.1339. Complete the series: AZ, BY, CX, DW, ? Ans: EV
Explanation: First letter: A, B, C, D, E (increasing by 1). Second letter: Z, Y, X, W, V (decreasing by 1). Next pair: E(5th letter) + V(22nd letter) = EV.
Trick: Two-letter series: check each position independently. If first goes forward (A→B→C) and second goes backward (Z→Y→X), continue both patterns separately.
Q.1340. Complete: Z, X, V, T, R, ? Ans: P
Explanation: Z(26), X(24), V(22), T(20), R(18), P(16). Each letter decreases by 2 positions. 18-2=16=P.
Trick: Convert letters to numbers to spot the pattern. If difference is constant (here -2), it's an arithmetic sequence in reverse alphabet.

9.14 Non-Verbal Reasoning Tips

Q.1341. In figure series, look for: Ans: Rotation, addition/deletion of elements, shading patterns, size changes.
Explanation: Figure series follow visual patterns. Common ones: (1) Shape rotates 45°/90° each step. (2) One element added each step. (3) Shading moves clockwise. (4) Shape grows/shrinks.
Trick: Check these in order: (1) Rotation? (2) Number of elements changing? (3) Shading/filling pattern? (4) Size change? (5) Combination of above? Usually one or two rules apply.
Q.1342. In paper folding problems, key concept: Ans: Holes appear symmetrically about fold lines when unfolded.
Explanation: When paper is folded and a hole is punched, unfolding creates symmetric holes on both sides of each fold line. If folded twice, you get 4 holes (2 per fold).
Trick: Count folds: 1 fold = 2 holes, 2 folds = 4 holes, 3 folds = 8 holes (doubles each fold). Holes mirror across each fold line. Unfold step by step from last fold to first.
Q.1343. In water image, the figure: Ans: Flips vertically (top becomes bottom, like a reflection in water below).
Explanation: Water reflection = upside down. The top part of the figure appears at the bottom in the reflection. Left-right remains the same.
Trick: Water image = vertical flip (↕). Mirror image = horizontal flip (↔). Don't confuse them! Water = up/down swap. Mirror = left/right swap.
Q.1344. In mirror image, the figure: Ans: Flips horizontally (left becomes right, like looking in a mirror).
Explanation: A mirror placed to the right of a figure swaps left and right. The top and bottom remain unchanged. Letters like b become d, p becomes q.
Trick: Mirror image: only left↔right changes. Letters that look same in mirror: A, H, I, M, O, T, U, V, W, X, Y. Letters that change: b↔d, p↔q, J↔L (reversed).
Q.1345. Embedded figures: Look for the given figure: Ans: Hidden within the complex figure, maintaining same size and orientation.
Explanation: The question figure is embedded (hidden) inside one of the answer figures. It must be the exact same shape, same size, same orientation — just surrounded by extra lines.
Trick: Trace the outline of the question figure with your finger on each option. The correct option will have the exact shape traceable without lifting your finger or changing direction.
Q.1346. Pattern completion: The missing piece should: Ans: Complete the overall pattern/design maintaining symmetry and continuity.
Explanation: The missing piece must fit seamlessly — lines should connect, patterns should continue, and the overall design should look complete and balanced.
Trick: Look at the edges of the missing piece area. The lines/patterns at those edges must match exactly with the answer option's edges. Eliminate options where lines don't connect.
Q.1347. Dot situation: A dot inside triangle but outside circle means: Ans: The element belongs to the category represented by triangle but NOT to the category represented by circle.
Explanation: In Venn diagram dot problems, each shape represents a category. A dot's position shows which categories it belongs to. Inside triangle = belongs to that group. Outside circle = doesn't belong to that group.
Trick: Check each shape the dot is inside of = belongs to ALL those categories. Outside of = doesn't belong to those. Example: dot inside triangle and circle but outside square = belongs to both triangle and circle categories but not square.
Q.1348. Figure matrix (3×3): Find pattern in rows and columns: Ans: Each row/column follows the same rule (rotation, element addition, etc.).
Explanation: In a 3×3 matrix, check: (1) Does each row follow a pattern? (2) Does each column follow a pattern? (3) Is there a diagonal pattern? Usually rows OR columns have consistent rules.
Trick: Common matrix rules: (1) Elements from fig 1 + fig 2 = fig 3. (2) Rotation increases across row. (3) Shading alternates. (4) XOR pattern (present in 1 and 2 but not 3, or vice versa).
Q.1349. Rule detection in figures: Common rules include: Ans: 90°/180°/270° rotation, element count increase, alternating patterns, reflection.
Explanation: Most common rules: Clockwise rotation (45°, 90°, 180°), adding one element per step, alternating between two states, growing/shrinking, and combining elements from previous figures.
Trick: Priority order to check: (1) Rotation — does the whole figure or part of it rotate? (2) Count — are elements being added/removed? (3) Alternation — does it flip between two states? (4) Movement — does something shift position?
Q.1350. Cube net: Which of the following can be folded into a cube? Ans: There are exactly 11 possible nets of a cube.
Explanation: A cube net is an arrangement of 6 connected squares that can fold into a cube. Out of all possible arrangements of 6 squares, only 11 unique shapes work. The most common: cross/plus shape, T-shape, L-shape, Z-shape, and straight line (1×6 doesn't work!).
Trick: Quick elimination: (1) If 4+ squares in a row with squares on same side = won't fold. (2) If any 2×2 block exists = won't fold. (3) A straight line of 5+ = won't fold. (4) Must have exactly 6 squares connected edge-to-edge.

9.15 Advanced Reasoning

Q.1351. If + means -, - means ×, × means ÷, ÷ means +, then 12+4-2×4÷1 = ? Ans: 11
Explanation: Replace symbols: 12-4×2÷4+1. Apply BODMAS: First ×: 4×2=8. Then ÷: 8÷4=2. Wait — need to be careful with order. 12-4×2÷4+1 → BODMAS: 4×2=8, then 8÷4=2, then 12-2+1=11.
Trick: Step 1: Replace ALL symbols first. Step 2: Write the new expression clearly. Step 3: Apply BODMAS strictly (÷ and × left to right first, then + and -).
Q.1352. How many meaningful words can be formed from AETL using each letter once? Ans: 2 (LATE, TALE)
Explanation: Possible 4-letter arrangements of A,E,T,L: Try meaningful English words: LATE ✓, TALE ✓, TEAL ✓ (actually 3!). Common exam answer considers LATE and TALE = 2. TEAL may also be accepted.
Trick: For "meaningful words" questions: mentally try common words with those letters. Start with common patterns (consonant-vowel-consonant-vowel). Don't count proper nouns.
Q.1353. Arrange in meaningful order: 1.Sentence 2.Word 3.Letter 4.Paragraph 5.Chapter Ans: 3, 2, 1, 4, 5 (Letter → Word → Sentence → Paragraph → Chapter)
Explanation: From smallest to largest unit of text: Letters combine to form Words, Words form Sentences, Sentences form Paragraphs, Paragraphs form Chapters.
Trick: "Meaningful order" usually means smallest→largest, or first→last in sequence. Think about what builds upon what.
Q.1354. Arrange: 1.Infant 2.Old age 3.Adolescence 4.Childhood 5.Adulthood Ans: 1, 4, 3, 5, 2 (Infant → Childhood → Adolescence → Adulthood → Old age)
Explanation: Life stages in chronological order: Birth/Infant (0-2) → Childhood (3-12) → Adolescence/Teenage (13-19) → Adulthood (20-60) → Old age (60+).
Trick: Life sequence problems: always arrange by age/time. Infant is always first, Old age is always last.
Q.1355. If the day before yesterday was Thursday, what day will it be day after tomorrow? Ans: Monday
Explanation: Day before yesterday = Thursday. So yesterday = Friday. Today = Saturday. Tomorrow = Sunday. Day after tomorrow = Monday.
Trick: Count from the given reference: "Day before yesterday" = Today - 2. "Day after tomorrow" = Today + 2. Total gap = 4 days. Thursday + 4 = Monday. Shortcut: just add 4 to the given day.
Q.1356. A clock shows 8:00. What is the time in mirror? Ans: 4:00 (subtract from 12:00 for mirror time, or 11:60 for times with minutes)
Q.1357. How many days are there from March 15 to June 20 (same year)? Ans: 97 days (Mar:16 + Apr:30 + May:31 + Jun:20 = 97)
Q.1358. If 1st January 2024 is Monday, what day is 1st January 2025? Ans: Wednesday (2024 is leap year = 2 odd days, Mon+2=Wed)
Q.1359. In how many ways can 5 people be seated in a row? Ans: 120 (5! = 5x4x3x2x1 = 120)
Q.1360. In how many ways can 5 people be seated in a circle? Ans: 24
Explanation: Circular permutation = (n-1)! = (5-1)! = 4! = 4×3×2×1 = 24. We fix one person's position and arrange the rest.
Trick: Linear arrangement = n! but circular = (n-1)! because rotations are considered same. For necklace/bracelet (flips also same): (n-1)!/2.
Q.1361. If ABCDEF is coded as BADCFE, then GHIJKL = ? Ans: HGJILK
Explanation: Pattern: swap adjacent pairs. AB→BA, CD→DC, EF→FE. Apply to GHIJKL: GH→HG, IJ→JI, KL→LK = HGJILK.
Trick: Pair-swap coding: take letters in pairs and reverse each pair. Very common pattern. Check: 1st↔2nd, 3rd↔4th, 5th↔6th.
Q.1362. Number of diagonals in a polygon with n sides: Ans: n(n-3)/2
Explanation: Each vertex connects to (n-3) other vertices via diagonals (can't connect to itself or 2 adjacent vertices). Total connections = n(n-3), but each diagonal counted twice, so divide by 2. Hexagon(6): 6×3/2 = 9.
Trick: Quick values: Triangle=0, Square=2, Pentagon=5, Hexagon=9, Octagon=20. Formula: n(n-3)/2.
Q.1363. Sum of angles of a polygon with n sides: Ans: (n-2) × 180°
Explanation: A polygon can be divided into (n-2) triangles. Each triangle has 180°. So total = (n-2)×180°. Triangle: 180°, Square: 360°, Pentagon: 540°, Hexagon: 720°.
Trick: Each interior angle of regular polygon = (n-2)×180/n. For hexagon: 720/6 = 120° each.
Q.1364. LCM of 12, 15, 20: Ans: 60
Explanation: Prime factorization: 12=2²×3, 15=3×5, 20=2²×5. LCM = highest power of each prime = 2²×3×5 = 4×3×5 = 60.
Trick: LCM = take highest power of every prime factor. HCF = take lowest power of common prime factors. LCM × HCF = Product (for two numbers only).
Q.1365. HCF of 36, 48, 60: Ans: 12
Explanation: 36=2²×3², 48=2⁴×3, 60=2²×3×5. HCF = lowest power of COMMON primes = 2²×3 = 4×3 = 12.
Trick: Quick method: Find HCF of first two (36,48)=12, then HCF of (12,60)=12. Or use successive division method.
Q.1366. If a number is divisible by both 3 and 4, it is divisible by: Ans: 12
Explanation: 3 and 4 are coprime (HCF=1). When two coprime numbers both divide N, their product also divides N. 3×4=12.
Trick: This works ONLY for coprime numbers. Divisible by 4 and 6 does NOT mean divisible by 24 (since HCF(4,6)=2, LCM=12). Always check if the numbers are coprime first.
Q.1367. Divisibility rule for 11: Ans: Difference of sum of alternate digits is 0 or divisible by 11.
Explanation: For 918082: Odd position digits (from right): 2+0+1=3. Even position digits: 8+8+9=25. Difference: |25-3|=22, divisible by 11 ✓.
Trick: "Alternate digit difference" rule. Sum digits at odd places, sum digits at even places, find difference. If 0 or multiple of 11 → divisible. Example: 121 → (1+1)-(2)=0 ✓.
Q.1368. Divisibility rule for 9: Ans: Sum of all digits is divisible by 9.
Explanation: For 729: 7+2+9=18, and 18÷9=2 ✓. For 123: 1+2+3=6, 6÷9≠whole number ✗.
Trick: Same rule works for 3 (sum divisible by 3). For 9, the sum must be divisible by 9 specifically. Quick: keep adding digits until single digit — if it's 9, divisible by 9.
Q.1369. Divisibility rule for 8: Ans: Last 3 digits form a number divisible by 8.
Explanation: For 1256: last 3 digits = 256. 256÷8=32 ✓. For 7140: last 3 digits = 140. 140÷8=17.5 ✗.
Trick: Divisibility rules by last digits: By 2→last 1 digit. By 4→last 2 digits. By 8→last 3 digits. By 16→last 4 digits. Pattern: 2ⁿ checks last n digits.
Q.1370. Square root of 1764: Ans: 42
Explanation: 1764: Last digit is 4, so root ends in 2 or 8. 40²=1600, 50²=2500. So between 40-50. Try 42²=1764 ✓.
Trick: For perfect squares: (1) Last digit tells possible root endings (0→0, 1→1/9, 4→2/8, 5→5, 6→4/6, 9→3/7). (2) Estimate range. (3) Try the candidate.
Q.1371. Cube root of 3375: Ans: 15
Explanation: 3375: Last digit 5 → cube root ends in 5. Remove last 3 digits: 3 remains. 1³=1, 2³=8. Since 1<3<8, tens digit = 1. So cube root = 15. Verify: 15³=3375 ✓.
Trick: Cube root shortcut: (1) Last digit of cube → last digit of root (0→0,1→1,2→8,3→7,4→4,5→5,6→6,7→3,8→2,9→9). (2) Remove last 3 digits, compare remainder with cubes to find tens digit.
Q.1372. If x% of 200 = 30, then x = ? Ans: 15
Explanation: x/100 × 200 = 30 → 2x = 30 → x = 15.
Trick: "x% of N = M" → x = (M/N)×100. Here: (30/200)×100 = 15. Or think: 200's 10% = 20, 15% = 30 ✓.
Q.1373. A number increased by 20% becomes 240. Original number: Ans: 200
Explanation: Original × 1.20 = 240. Original = 240/1.20 = 200. Verify: 200 + 20% of 200 = 200+40 = 240 ✓.
Trick: "Increased by x%" → multiply by (100+x)/100. To find original: divide result by (100+x)/100. "Decreased by x%" → multiply by (100-x)/100.
Q.1374. If CP = 800, SP = 920, Profit% = ? Ans: 15%
Explanation: Profit = SP - CP = 920-800 = 120. Profit% = (Profit/CP)×100 = (120/800)×100 = 15%.
Trick: Profit% always calculated on CP. Loss% also on CP. Shortcut: (SP-CP)/CP × 100. If SP>CP → profit. If CP>SP → loss.
Q.1375. Successive discounts of 10% and 20% on Rs.1000: Ans: Rs.720
Explanation: After 10% discount: 1000 × 0.90 = 900. After 20% discount on 900: 900 × 0.80 = 720. Final price = Rs.720.
Trick: Successive discounts: multiply multipliers. 0.9 × 0.8 = 0.72. So effective discount = 28% (not 30%!). Formula: Effective discount = a+b-ab/100 = 10+20-200/100 = 28%.
Q.1376. Average speed for equal distances at 40 km/h and 60 km/h: Ans: 48 km/h
Explanation: For equal distances, average speed = Harmonic mean = 2×S1×S2/(S1+S2) = 2×40×60/(40+60) = 4800/100 = 48 km/h. NOT simple average (50)!
Trick: Equal distance → use Harmonic mean: 2ab/(a+b). Equal time → use Arithmetic mean: (a+b)/2. This is the most common mistake in speed problems — always check if distance or time is equal.
Q.1377. A boat's speed in still water is 10 km/h, stream speed is 2 km/h. Downstream speed: Ans: Downstream = 12 km/h, Upstream = 8 km/h
Explanation: Downstream (with current): Boat + Stream = 10+2 = 12 km/h. Upstream (against current): Boat - Stream = 10-2 = 8 km/h.
Trick: Speed in still water = (Downstream + Upstream)/2. Stream speed = (Downstream - Upstream)/2. These two formulas solve ALL boat problems.
Q.1378. Partnership: A invests Rs.5000 for 12 months, B invests Rs.6000 for 10 months. Profit ratio: Ans: 1:1
Explanation: Profit ratio = Investment × Time. A: 5000×12 = 60000. B: 6000×10 = 60000. Ratio = 60000:60000 = 1:1.
Trick: Partnership profit sharing: Capital × Time. If same time → ratio of capitals. If same capital → ratio of time. If both different → multiply each.
Q.1379. Mixture: 20 liters of mixture has milk:water = 3:1. How much water to add to make ratio 3:2? Ans: 5 liters
Explanation: Milk = 3/4 × 20 = 15L. Water = 1/4 × 20 = 5L. New ratio 3:2 means milk:water = 15:x where 15/x = 3/2 → x = 10. Water needed = 10-5 = 5L to add.
Trick: In mixture problems, the component NOT being added stays constant. Here milk stays 15L. Use the new ratio to find required water, then subtract existing water.
Q.1380. Permutation nPr formula: Ans: n!/(n-r)!
Explanation: nPr = number of ways to arrange r items from n items where ORDER MATTERS. 5P3 = 5!/(5-3)! = 120/2 = 60.
Trick: Permutation = arrangement (order matters). Think "President, VP, Secretary" — different positions. nPr = n × (n-1) × (n-2) × ... (r terms).
Q.1381. Combination nCr formula: Ans: n!/[r!(n-r)!]
Explanation: nCr = number of ways to SELECT r items from n where ORDER DOESN'T MATTER. 5C3 = 5!/(3!×2!) = 120/12 = 10.
Trick: Combination = selection (order doesn't matter). Think "choose 3 members for committee" — no positions. nCr = nPr/r!. Always: nCr ≤ nPr.
Q.1382. Probability of getting sum 7 with two dice: Ans: 6/36 = 1/6
Explanation: Total outcomes with 2 dice = 6×6 = 36. Favorable outcomes for sum 7: (1,6),(2,5),(3,4),(4,3),(5,2),(6,1) = 6 ways. P = 6/36 = 1/6.
Trick: Sum 7 has MOST combinations (6 ways) of any sum with 2 dice. Sum 2 and Sum 12 have least (1 way each). Memorize: Sum 7 = 6/36 = 1/6.
Q.1383. If two events are independent, P(A and B) = ? Ans: P(A) × P(B)
Explanation: Independent events don't affect each other. The probability of both happening = product of individual probabilities. Example: P(Head AND 6 on die) = 1/2 × 1/6 = 1/12.
Trick: Independent → multiply. "AND" = multiply. "OR" (mutually exclusive) = add. P(A or B) = P(A)+P(B)-P(A and B).
Q.1384. Mean of first n natural numbers: Ans: (n+1)/2
Explanation: Sum of first n natural numbers = n(n+1)/2. Mean = Sum/n = [n(n+1)/2]/n = (n+1)/2. For n=10: mean = 11/2 = 5.5.
Trick: Mean of 1 to n = (first+last)/2 = (1+n)/2. This works for any arithmetic sequence: mean = (first+last)/2.
Q.1385. Sum of first n natural numbers: Ans: n(n+1)/2
Explanation: 1+2+3+...+n = n(n+1)/2. For n=100: 100×101/2 = 5050. Gauss discovered this as a child!
Trick: Quick mental math: pair first and last (1+100=101), count pairs (50 pairs), multiply: 50×101=5050. Works for any AP sum.
Q.1386. Sum of squares of first n natural numbers: Ans: n(n+1)(2n+1)/6
Explanation: 1²+2²+3²+...+n² = n(n+1)(2n+1)/6. For n=10: 10×11×21/6 = 2310/6 = 385.
Trick: Memorize three sum formulas: Σn = n(n+1)/2, Σn² = n(n+1)(2n+1)/6, Σn³ = [n(n+1)/2]² (cube sum = square of natural sum!).
Q.1387. Area of circle: Ans: πr²
Explanation: π ≈ 3.14159 or 22/7. For r=7: Area = 22/7 × 49 = 154 sq units.
Trick: Use π=22/7 when radius is multiple of 7 (easier calculation). Use π=3.14 otherwise. Area quadruples when radius doubles (proportional to r²).
Q.1388. Circumference of circle: Ans: 2πr (or πd where d=diameter)
Explanation: For r=7: C = 2×22/7×7 = 44 units. Circumference is the perimeter (distance around) the circle.
Trick: Circumference doubles when radius doubles (proportional to r). Area quadruples. Remember: C=πd is sometimes easier than 2πr.
Q.1389. Volume of sphere: Ans: (4/3)πr³
Explanation: For r=3: V = 4/3 × 22/7 × 27 = 113.14 cubic units. Volume increases 8× when radius doubles (proportional to r³).
Trick: Sphere formulas: Surface area = 4πr², Volume = (4/3)πr³. Hemisphere: Curved SA = 2πr², Total SA = 3πr², Volume = (2/3)πr³.
Q.1390. Volume of cylinder: Ans: πr²h
Explanation: Cylinder = circle × height. Base area = πr², multiply by height h. For r=7, h=10: V = 22/7 × 49 × 10 = 1540 cubic units.
Trick: Cylinder: Volume=πr²h, Curved SA=2πrh, Total SA=2πr(r+h). Think of it as a "stretched circle."
Q.1391. Volume of cone: Ans: (1/3)πr²h
Explanation: Cone = 1/3 of cylinder with same base and height. If cylinder holds 300ml, cone with same r and h holds 100ml.
Trick: Cone = (1/3) × Cylinder. Sphere = (2/3) × Cylinder (for cylinder with h=2r). These ratios are exam favorites.
Q.1392. Pythagoras theorem: Ans: Hypotenuse² = Base² + Perpendicular² (or c²=a²+b²)
Explanation: In a right-angled triangle, the square of the longest side (hypotenuse) equals the sum of squares of the other two sides. Only works for RIGHT triangles.
Trick: If given two sides, find third: c=√(a²+b²) for hypotenuse, a=√(c²-b²) for a leg. Check if triangle is right-angled: if a²+b²=c², it's a right triangle.
Q.1393. Common Pythagorean triplets: Ans: (3,4,5), (5,12,13), (8,15,17), (7,24,25)
Explanation: These are sets of 3 integers where a²+b²=c². 3²+4²=9+16=25=5² ✓. Any multiple also works: (6,8,10), (9,12,15), (15,20,25).
Trick: Memorize base triplets and their multiples. If you see 6 and 8 in a problem, hypotenuse = 10 (2× the 3,4,5 triplet). Saves calculation time in exams.
Q.1394. Area of triangle: Ans: (1/2) x base x height
Q.1395. Area of equilateral triangle with side a: Ans: (sqrt(3)/4) x a^2
Q.1396. Heron's formula for area of triangle: Ans: sqrt[s(s-a)(s-b)(s-c)] where s=(a+b+c)/2
Q.1397. Sum of interior angles of a triangle: Ans: 180 degrees
Q.1398. Exterior angle of a triangle equals: Ans: Sum of two opposite interior angles
Q.1399. In a right triangle, the longest side is: Ans: Hypotenuse (opposite to 90 degree angle)
Q.1400. If two angles of a triangle are 60 and 70 degrees, third angle is: Ans: 50 degrees (180-60-70=50)

Section 10: Data Interpretation & Quantitative Aptitude (100 Questions)

Topics: Tables, Bar Graphs, Pie Charts, Line Graphs, Percentage, Ratio, Average, Time & Work, Time & Distance, Profit & Loss

10.1 Data Interpretation Concepts

Q.1401. In a pie chart, total angle represents: Ans: 360 degrees (full circle)
Q.1402. If a sector represents 25%, its angle is: Ans: 90 degrees (25% of 360)
Q.1403. Percentage increase formula: Ans: [(New - Old)/Old] x 100
Q.1404. Percentage decrease formula: Ans: [(Old - New)/Old] x 100
Q.1405. If population increases from 1000 to 1200, percentage increase: Ans: 20% [(1200-1000)/1000 x 100]
Q.1406. CAGR (Compound Annual Growth Rate) formula: Ans: [(Final/Initial)^(1/n) - 1] x 100
Q.1407. In a bar graph, the height of bar represents: Ans: The value/quantity of the data
Q.1408. In a line graph, the slope indicates: Ans: Rate of change (steeper = faster change)
Q.1409. To find what percentage A is of B: Ans: (A/B) x 100
Q.1410. If A is 20% more than B, then B is what % less than A? Ans: 16.67% [20/120 x 100 = 16.67%]

10.2 Averages & Central Tendency

Q.1411. Average (Mean) = Ans: Sum of observations / Number of observations
Q.1412. Median of 3, 7, 2, 9, 5 is: Ans: 5 (arrange: 2,3,5,7,9; middle value = 5)
Q.1413. Mode is: Ans: Most frequently occurring value
Q.1414. Weighted average formula: Ans: Sum(value x weight) / Sum(weights)
Q.1415. If average of 10 numbers is 15, and one number 20 is removed, new average: Ans: (150-20)/9 = 130/9 = 14.44
Q.1416. Average of first 10 natural numbers: Ans: 5.5 [(1+10)/2 = 5.5]
Q.1417. Average of first 10 even numbers (2,4,...20): Ans: 11 [(2+20)/2 = 11]
Q.1418. If a batsman's average after 20 innings is 30, and he scores 80 in 21st innings, new average: Ans: (600+80)/21 = 680/21 = 32.38
Q.1419. Range = Ans: Maximum value - Minimum value
Q.1420. Standard Deviation measures: Ans: Spread/dispersion of data from the mean

10.3 Ratio, Proportion & Partnership

Q.1421. If A:B = 3:4 and B:C = 5:6, then A:B:C = ? Ans: 15:20:24
Q.1422. Divide Rs.1200 in ratio 2:3:5. Shares are: Ans: Rs.240, Rs.360, Rs.600
Q.1423. If 4:x = x:9, then x = ? Ans: 6 (x^2 = 36, x = 6)
Q.1424. Third proportional to 4 and 12 is: Ans: 36 (4:12 = 12:x, x=36)
Q.1425. Mean proportional of 4 and 16 is: Ans: 8 (sqrt(4x16) = sqrt(64) = 8)

10.4 Time, Speed & Distance

Q.1426. Speed = Distance/Time. Convert 72 km/h to m/s: Ans: 20 m/s (multiply by 5/18)
Q.1427. Convert 15 m/s to km/h: Ans: 54 km/h (multiply by 18/5)
Q.1428. Relative speed of two objects moving in same direction: Ans: Difference of speeds
Q.1429. Relative speed of two objects moving in opposite directions: Ans: Sum of speeds
Q.1430. A car covers 300 km in 5 hours. Speed: Ans: 60 km/h
Q.1431. Time taken to cover 450 km at 75 km/h: Ans: 6 hours
Q.1432. If a man walks at 5 km/h, he reaches 10 min late. At 6 km/h, he reaches 10 min early. Distance: Ans: 10 km (D/5 - D/6 = 20/60; D/30 = 1/3; D=10)
Q.1433. Two trains 100m and 150m long, moving in same direction at 60 and 40 km/h. Time to cross: Ans: 250/(20x5/18) = 250/(50/9) = 45 seconds
Q.1434. A man rows 12 km downstream in 2 hours and 12 km upstream in 3 hours. Speed of stream: Ans: Downstream=6, Upstream=4; Stream=(6-4)/2=1 km/h
Q.1435. Circular track: A and B start from same point. A completes round in 4 min, B in 6 min. They meet at start after: Ans: LCM(4,6) = 12 minutes

10.5 Time & Work

Q.1436. If A does work in 10 days, A's one day work: Ans: 1/10
Q.1437. A does in 12 days, B in 18 days. Together: Ans: 1/12+1/18 = 5/36; Days = 36/5 = 7.2 days
Q.1438. A does in 10 days, B in 15 days. They work together for 4 days, remaining work: Ans: 4(1/10+1/15) = 4(1/6) = 2/3 done; Remaining = 1/3
Q.1439. 10 men can do work in 20 days. 15 men can do in: Ans: 200/15 = 13.33 days (Men x Days = constant)
Q.1440. If 5 men or 8 women can do work in 12 days, 2 men and 3 women can do in: Ans: 5M=8W in 12 days; 1M=8/5 W; 2M+3W = 16/5+3 = 31/5 W; 8W x 12 = 96 W-days; Time = 96/(31/5) = 480/31 = 15.48 days

10.6 Profit, Loss & Discount

Q.1441. Profit% = [(SP-CP)/CP] × 100. If CP=400, SP=500: Ans: 25%
Explanation: Profit = 500-400 = 100. Profit% = (100/400)×100 = 25%.
Trick: Quick fraction: 100/400 = 1/4 = 25%. Train yourself to see common fractions: 1/4=25%, 1/5=20%, 1/3=33.3%, 1/8=12.5%.
Q.1442. Loss% = [(CP-SP)/CP] × 100. If CP=500, SP=400: Ans: 20%
Explanation: Loss = 500-400 = 100. Loss% = (100/500)×100 = 20%.
Trick: Both profit% and loss% are calculated on CP (Cost Price), never on SP. This is the most common exam trap.
Q.1443. If SP = 2 × CP, then profit%: Ans: 100%
Explanation: SP=2CP. Profit = SP-CP = 2CP-CP = CP. Profit% = (CP/CP)×100 = 100%. You doubled your money.
Trick: SP=2×CP → 100% profit. SP=3×CP → 200% profit. SP=CP/2 → 50% loss. Quick multiplier shortcuts.
Q.1444. Marked Price, Discount, SP relationship: Ans: SP = MP - Discount; Discount% = [(MP-SP)/MP] × 100
Explanation: MP (Marked Price) is the listed/tag price. Discount is given on MP. SP (Selling Price) = what customer actually pays. Profit is calculated on CP, Discount on MP.
Trick: Chain: CP → (markup%) → MP → (discount%) → SP → (compare with CP) → Profit/Loss%. Each step uses a different base!
Q.1445. If MP=1000, Discount=20%, SP: Ans: Rs.800
Explanation: Discount amount = 20% of 1000 = 200. SP = MP - Discount = 1000-200 = 800. Or directly: SP = 1000 × 0.80 = 800.
Trick: SP = MP × (100-Discount%)/100. Multiply by the "remaining percentage" directly.
Q.1446. Two successive discounts of 20% and 10% equivalent single discount: Ans: 28%
Explanation: After 20%: price = 0.80 × original. After 10% more: 0.80 × 0.90 = 0.72 × original. Effective discount = 1-0.72 = 0.28 = 28%.
Trick: Formula: Effective discount = a + b - ab/100. Here: 20+10-200/100 = 30-2 = 28%. NOT simply 30%! Successive discounts are always less than their sum.
Q.1447. If an article is sold at 10% profit and 10% loss, net result: Ans: 1% loss
Explanation: When same % profit and loss are involved in buying/selling: Net loss% = (common%)²/100 = 10²/100 = 1% loss. Always a loss, never profit!
Trick: "Buy at x% profit, sell at x% loss" (or vice versa) → Always net loss = x²/100 %. This is a classic exam question. 10%→1% loss, 20%→4% loss, 25%→6.25% loss.
Q.1448. CP of article using SP and Profit%: Ans: CP = SP × 100/(100+Profit%)
Explanation: If SP=1200 and Profit=20%: CP = 1200 × 100/120 = 1000. Verify: 1000+20%=1200 ✓.
Trick: CP from SP: Profit → divide by (100+P%)/100. Loss → divide by (100-L%)/100. Think: SP = CP × multiplier, so CP = SP / multiplier.
Q.1449. If by selling 12 articles, profit = SP of 4 articles, profit%: Ans: 50%
Explanation: Let SP of each = Rs.1. Revenue from 12 = Rs.12. Profit = SP of 4 = Rs.4. So CP of 12 = Revenue - Profit = 12-4 = 8. Profit% = (4/8)×100 = 50%.
Trick: "Profit = SP of x articles out of y sold" → Profit% = [x/(y-x)] × 100. Here: 4/(12-4) × 100 = 4/8 × 100 = 50%.
Q.1450. Break-even point is when: Ans: Total Revenue = Total Cost (no profit, no loss)
Explanation: At break-even: Revenue exactly covers all costs (fixed + variable). Profit = 0. Any sale beyond this point generates profit.
Trick: Break-even units = Fixed Cost / (SP per unit - Variable Cost per unit). This is the minimum you must sell to not lose money.

10.7 Simple & Compound Interest

Q.1451. SI on Rs.5000 at 8% for 3 years: Ans: Rs.1200
Explanation: SI = P×R×T/100 = 5000×8×3/100 = 120000/100 = 1200.
Trick: Break it down: 8% of 5000 = 400 per year. For 3 years = 400×3 = 1200. Calculating yearly SI first is often easier mentally.
Q.1452. CI on Rs.10000 at 10% for 2 years: Ans: Rs.2100
Explanation: A = P(1+R/100)^T = 10000×(1.1)² = 10000×1.21 = 12100. CI = A-P = 12100-10000 = 2100.
Trick: For 2 years: CI = SI + SI on SI of first year. SI for 2 years = 2000. Extra = 10% of 1000 (first year's interest) = 100. CI = 2000+100 = 2100.
Q.1453. Difference between CI and SI for 2 years: Ans: P×(R/100)²
Explanation: CI-SI for 2 years = P×(R/100)². For P=10000, R=10: Diff = 10000×(0.1)² = 10000×0.01 = 100. (CI=2100, SI=2000, diff=100 ✓).
Trick: This formula only works for exactly 2 years. For 3 years: CI-SI = P×(R/100)²×(3+R/100). The 2-year formula is most commonly asked.
Q.1454. A sum doubles in 8 years at SI. Rate: Ans: 12.5%
Explanation: Doubles means SI = P. P = P×R×8/100 → 100 = 8R → R = 12.5%.
Trick: "Doubles in T years at SI" → Rate = 100/T. Triples → Rate = 200/T. Quadruples → Rate = 300/T. Formula: Rate = (Multiple-1)×100/T.
Q.1455. Rule of 72: Time to double money at CI: Ans: Time ≈ 72/Rate
Explanation: At 8% CI, money doubles in ≈ 72/8 = 9 years. At 12% CI, doubles in ≈ 72/12 = 6 years. This is an approximation.
Trick: Rule of 72 for doubling, Rule of 114 for tripling, Rule of 144 for quadrupling. Quick mental math for CI problems.
Q.1456. At 10% CI, Rs.1000 becomes after 3 years: Ans: Rs.1331
Explanation: A = 1000×(1.1)³ = 1000×1.331 = 1331. Year by year: 1000→1100→1210→1331.
Trick: (1.1)² = 1.21, (1.1)³ = 1.331, (1.1)⁴ = 1.4641. Memorize these powers of 1.1 — they appear constantly in CI problems.
Q.1457. If SI on a sum for 4 years at 5% is Rs.400, the sum is: Ans: Rs.2000
Explanation: SI = P×R×T/100 → 400 = P×5×4/100 → 400 = P×20/100 → 400 = P/5 → P = 2000.
Trick: Rearrange SI formula: P = SI×100/(R×T) = 400×100/(5×4) = 40000/20 = 2000.
Q.1458. Effective rate when compounded half-yearly at 10%: Ans: 10.25%
Explanation: Half-yearly rate = 10/2 = 5%. Effective annual rate = (1+0.05)²-1 = 1.1025-1 = 0.1025 = 10.25%.
Trick: Compounded n times/year at R%: Effective rate = (1+R/100n)^n - 1. Half-yearly(n=2), Quarterly(n=4), Monthly(n=12). More frequent compounding → higher effective rate.
Q.1459. Present value of Rs.1210 due in 2 years at 10% CI: Ans: Rs.1000
Explanation: PV = Amount/(1+R/100)^T = 1210/(1.1)² = 1210/1.21 = 1000.
Trick: Present Value = Future Value ÷ (1+r)^n. It's the reverse of CI calculation. "What amount today will grow to X in n years?"
Q.1460. EMI stands for: Ans: Equated Monthly Installment
Explanation: EMI is a fixed monthly payment made to repay a loan over a set period. It includes both principal repayment and interest. Early EMIs have more interest; later ones have more principal.
Trick: EMI ≈ P×R×(1+R)^N / [(1+R)^N - 1] where R=monthly rate, N=months. For exams, usually just the full form is asked.

10.8 Number System & Simplification

Q.1461. Types of numbers: Natural, Whole, Integer, Rational, Irrational, Real: Ans: N ⊂ W ⊂ Z ⊂ Q; Q ∪ Irrational = Real
Explanation: Natural (1,2,3...) ⊂ Whole (0,1,2...) ⊂ Integers (...-2,-1,0,1,2...) ⊂ Rational (p/q form) ⊂ Real. Irrational numbers (√2, π) fill the gaps between rationals.
Trick: Remember: N⊂W⊂Z⊂Q⊂R. "Naughty Worms In Quiet Rooms" — Natural, Whole, Integer, Rational, Real. Irrational is separate but part of Real.
Q.1462. Rational number: Can be expressed as p/q where q≠0: Ans: Examples: 1/2, 3, -4, 0.5, 0.333... (terminating or repeating decimals)
Explanation: Any number that can be written as a fraction of two integers is rational. Includes all integers (3=3/1), terminating decimals (0.5=1/2), and repeating decimals (0.333...=1/3).
Trick: If decimal terminates or repeats → rational. If it goes on forever without repeating → irrational. 0.101001000100001... = irrational (pattern but no repetition).
Q.1463. Irrational number examples: Ans: √2, √3, π, e (non-terminating, non-repeating decimals)
Explanation: These cannot be expressed as p/q. √2=1.41421356... (never repeats). π=3.14159265... (never repeats). They exist "between" rational numbers on the number line.
Trick: √(perfect square) = rational (√4=2, √9=3). √(non-perfect square) = irrational (√2, √3, √5, √7). π and e are always irrational.
Q.1464. BODMAS rule order: Ans: Brackets, Orders (powers/roots), Division, Multiplication, Addition, Subtraction
Explanation: Solve in this order: First brackets (), then powers/roots, then ÷ and × (left to right), then + and - (left to right). Division and Multiplication have EQUAL priority (left to right).
Trick: "Big Old Dogs Make Awful Sounds" = BODMAS. Note: × and ÷ are same priority (left to right), + and - are same priority (left to right). Don't always do ÷ before ×!
Q.1465. (a+b)² = Ans: a² + 2ab + b²
Explanation: (a+b)(a+b) = a²+ab+ab+b² = a²+2ab+b². Example: (3+2)²=25, and 9+12+4=25 ✓.
Trick: "Square first, square last, twice the product in between." Quick: 101² = (100+1)² = 10000+200+1 = 10201.
Q.1466. (a-b)² = Ans: a² - 2ab + b²
Explanation: (a-b)(a-b) = a²-ab-ab+b² = a²-2ab+b². Example: (5-2)²=9, and 25-20+4=9 ✓.
Trick: Same as (a+b)² but middle term is negative. Quick: 99² = (100-1)² = 10000-200+1 = 9801.
Q.1467. a² - b² = Ans: (a+b)(a-b)
Explanation: Difference of squares factorizes into sum × difference. Example: 25-9 = (5+3)(5-3) = 8×2 = 16 ✓.
Trick: Use for quick multiplication: 47×53 = (50-3)(50+3) = 50²-3² = 2500-9 = 2491. Very useful for mental math!
Q.1468. (a+b)³ = Ans: a³ + 3a²b + 3ab² + b³
Explanation: Or: a³ + b³ + 3ab(a+b). Example: (2+1)³=27, and 8+12+6+1=27 ✓.
Trick: Coefficients follow Pascal's triangle: 1,3,3,1. Powers of a decrease (3,2,1,0), powers of b increase (0,1,2,3).
Q.1469. If a+b+c=0, then a³+b³+c³ = Ans: 3abc
Explanation: Identity: a³+b³+c³-3abc = (a+b+c)(a²+b²+c²-ab-bc-ca). If a+b+c=0, right side=0, so a³+b³+c³=3abc.
Trick: This is a very frequently asked identity. If sum=0, cube sum=3×product. Example: 1+2+(-3)=0, so 1+8-27=3×1×2×(-3)=-18 ✓.
Q.1470. Remainder when 2^10 is divided by 7: Ans: 2
Explanation: 2¹=2, 2²=4, 2³=8÷7 rem 1. Since 2³ gives remainder 1, the pattern repeats every 3 powers. 2^10 = 2^9 × 2¹. 2^9=(2³)³ gives remainder 1³=1. So 2^10 gives remainder 1×2=2.
Trick: Find the cycle of remainders. Here 2^3≡1(mod 7), so cycle=3. Divide exponent by cycle: 10÷3=3 remainder 1. Answer = remainder of 2¹÷7 = 2. This "cyclicity" method works for all such problems.

10.9 Geometry & Mensuration Formulas

Q.1471. Area of rectangle: Ans: length × breadth
Explanation: Rectangle with l=8, b=5: Area = 8×5 = 40 sq units.
Trick: Rectangle: Area=l×b, Perimeter=2(l+b), Diagonal=√(l²+b²). All three formulas are frequently asked together.
Q.1472. Perimeter of rectangle: Ans: 2(length + breadth)
Explanation: Perimeter = sum of all sides = l+b+l+b = 2(l+b). For l=8, b=5: P = 2(13) = 26 units.
Trick: Perimeter = "fence around the shape." Area = "carpet inside the shape." Don't confuse them — perimeter is in units, area in square units.
Q.1473. Area of square with side a: Ans: a²
Explanation: Square is a special rectangle where l=b=a. Area = a×a = a². For side=7: Area = 49 sq units.
Trick: Square: Area=a², Perimeter=4a, Diagonal=a√2. If diagonal is given: side = diagonal/√2, area = diagonal²/2.
Q.1474. Diagonal of square with side a: Ans: a√2
Explanation: Using Pythagoras: diagonal² = a²+a² = 2a². Diagonal = √(2a²) = a√2. For side=10: diagonal = 10√2 ≈ 14.14.
Trick: √2 ≈ 1.414. So diagonal ≈ side × 1.414. Quick: side 7 → diagonal ≈ 9.9. Side 5 → diagonal ≈ 7.07.
Q.1475. Area of trapezium: Ans: ½ × (sum of parallel sides) × height
Explanation: Trapezium has two parallel sides (a and b) and height h between them. Area = ½(a+b)×h. If a=8, b=12, h=5: Area = ½×20×5 = 50.
Trick: Think of it as "average of parallel sides × height." The ½(a+b) is just the average of the two parallel sides.
Q.1476. Area of rhombus: Ans: ½ × d₁ × d₂ (half the product of diagonals)
Explanation: Rhombus diagonals bisect each other at right angles. Area = ½×d₁×d₂. If d₁=10, d₂=8: Area = ½×10×8 = 40.
Trick: Rhombus: Area = ½×d₁×d₂ OR side²×sin(angle). Also: side = ½√(d₁²+d₂²). All sides are equal in a rhombus.
Q.1477. Surface area of sphere: Ans: 4πr²
Explanation: Surface area = 4πr². For r=7: SA = 4×22/7×49 = 616 sq units. It's exactly 4 times the area of its great circle (πr²).
Trick: Sphere SA = 4 circles. Hemisphere curved SA = 2 circles. Hemisphere total SA = 3 circles (curved + flat base).
Q.1478. Curved surface area of cylinder: Ans: 2πrh
Explanation: If you "unroll" a cylinder, the curved surface becomes a rectangle with width=2πr (circumference) and height=h. Area = 2πr×h.
Trick: CSA = circumference × height = 2πrh. Think of wrapping a label around a can — the label is a rectangle.
Q.1479. Total surface area of cylinder: Ans: 2πr(r+h)
Explanation: TSA = Curved surface + 2 circular bases = 2πrh + 2πr² = 2πr(h+r).
Trick: TSA = CSA + 2 circles. Open cylinder (no top) = CSA + 1 circle = 2πrh + πr². Hollow cylinder has inner and outer surfaces.
Q.1480. Slant height of cone: Ans: l = √(r² + h²)
Explanation: The slant height is the distance from the tip to any point on the base circle's edge. By Pythagoras: l² = r² + h². For r=3, h=4: l=5.
Trick: Cone is basically a right triangle rotated. The slant height is the hypotenuse. If you see r=3, h=4, immediately know l=5 (3-4-5 triplet).
Q.1481. Volume of cuboid: Ans: length × breadth × height
Explanation: Cuboid (box shape) volume = l×b×h. For 5×4×3: V = 60 cubic units.
Trick: Cuboid: V=lbh, TSA=2(lb+bh+hl), Diagonal=√(l²+b²+h²). Cube is special case where l=b=h=a: V=a³, TSA=6a², Diagonal=a√3.
Q.1482. Diagonal of cuboid: Ans: √(l² + b² + h²)
Explanation: Space diagonal (corner to opposite corner) uses 3D Pythagoras. For 3×4×5: d = √(9+16+25) = √50 = 5√2.
Trick: Face diagonal = √(l²+b²) (2D). Space diagonal = √(l²+b²+h²) (3D). For cube with side a: face diagonal=a√2, space diagonal=a√3.
Q.1483. If radius of circle is doubled, area becomes: Ans: 4 times (area ∝ r²)
Explanation: Original area = πr². New area = π(2r)² = 4πr². Ratio = 4. Area quadruples when radius doubles.
Trick: Area ∝ r² (square relationship). Double r → 4× area. Triple r → 9× area. Half r → ¼ area. This applies to all circular/spherical areas.
Q.1484. If radius of sphere is tripled, volume becomes: Ans: 27 times (volume ∝ r³)
Explanation: Original volume = (4/3)πr³. New = (4/3)π(3r)³ = 27×(4/3)πr³. Volume becomes 27 times.
Trick: Volume ∝ r³ (cube relationship). Double r → 8× volume. Triple r → 27× volume. This applies to all 3D shapes when all dimensions scale equally.
Q.1485. Area of sector with angle θ: Ans: (θ/360) × πr²
Explanation: A sector is a "pizza slice" of a circle. Its area is proportional to its angle. For θ=90°, r=14: Area = (90/360)×(22/7)×196 = ¼×616 = 154.
Trick: Sector area = (angle/360)×full circle area. Arc length = (angle/360)×circumference. Same fraction applies to both.

10.10 Miscellaneous Quantitative

Q.1486. Allegation rule for mixtures: Ans: (Cheaper qty)/(Dearer qty) = (Dearer price - Mean)/(Mean - Cheaper price)
Explanation: Used to find ratio of mixing two items at different prices to get a desired average price. Draw a cross: put prices on left, mean in center, differences on right (cross-subtract).
Trick: Alligation cross method: Write cheaper and dearer on left, mean in middle. Cross-subtract to get ratio on right. This works for prices, concentrations, speeds, ages — any weighted average.
Q.1487. Clock gains/loses: If a clock gains x minutes per hour, in T hours it gains: Ans: x×T minutes
Explanation: Gain is linear. If gains 5 min/hour, in 12 hours gains 60 min = 1 hour. Clock shows 1 hour ahead of actual time.
Trick: Faulty clock problems: Real time elapsed = Clock time shown ± gain/loss. If clock gains, it shows MORE time than actual. If loses, shows LESS.
Q.1488. Pipes and Cisterns: If pipe A fills in x hours and B empties in y hours (y>x), net fill rate: Ans: 1/x - 1/y per hour
Explanation: A fills 1/x per hour (positive). B empties 1/y per hour (negative). Net = 1/x - 1/y. If x=6, y=8: Net = 1/6-1/8 = 1/24. Tank fills in 24 hours.
Trick: If y>x (fill faster than empty), tank eventually fills. If y
Q.1489. Calendar: 400 years have exactly: Ans: 0 odd days
Explanation: 400 years = 97 leap years + 303 ordinary years. Odd days = 97×2 + 303×1 = 194+303 = 497. 497÷7 = 71 weeks + 0 days. So 400 years = 0 odd days.
Trick: Memorize: 100 years=5 odd days, 200=3, 300=1, 400=0. Then cycle repeats. This is essential for "what day was it on..." questions.
Q.1490. Races: If A gives B a start of 10m in 100m race, it means: Ans: B starts 10m ahead; A runs 100m while B runs 90m from the same finish line.
Explanation: A starts at 0m, B starts at 10m. Both finish at 100m. So A covers 100m, B covers only 90m. A is faster — the head start compensates for B's slower speed.
Trick: "A gives B 10m start in 100m" = A runs 100m in same time B runs 90m. Speed ratio = 100:90 = 10:9. "A beats B by 10m" = when A finishes 100m, B has only covered 90m.
Q.1491. Geometric Progression sum (infinite, |r|<1): Ans: S = a/(1-r)
Explanation: When |r|<1, the terms get smaller and smaller, approaching 0. The sum converges to a/(1-r). Example: 1+1/2+1/4+1/8+... = 1/(1-0.5) = 2.
Trick: Only works when |r|<1 (terms shrink). If |r|≥1, infinite GP sum doesn't exist (diverges). Finite GP sum (n terms) = a(1-rⁿ)/(1-r).
Q.1492. Arithmetic Progression nth term: Ans: aₙ = a + (n-1)d
Explanation: a=first term, d=common difference. 5th term of AP 3,7,11...: a₅ = 3+(5-1)×4 = 3+16 = 19.
Trick: AP: constant difference between consecutive terms. To find any term: start + (how many jumps)×(jump size). "How many jumps" = n-1 (not n!).
Q.1493. AP sum of n terms: Ans: Sₙ = n/2 × [2a + (n-1)d] OR n/2 × (first + last)
Explanation: Sum of 3,7,11,15,19 (5 terms): S = 5/2 × (3+19) = 5/2 × 22 = 55. Or: 5/2 × [2×3+4×4] = 5/2×22 = 55.
Trick: Easier formula: S = n × (first+last)/2 = number of terms × average. The average of an AP is always (first+last)/2.
Q.1494. GP nth term: Ans: aₙ = a × r^(n-1)
Explanation: a=first term, r=common ratio. 5th term of GP 2,6,18...: a₅ = 2×3⁴ = 2×81 = 162.
Trick: GP: constant ratio between consecutive terms. Each term = previous × r. To find nth term: multiply first term by r, (n-1) times.
Q.1495. log(ab) = Ans: log a + log b
Explanation: Logarithm of a product = sum of logarithms. log(2×3) = log 2 + log 3 = 0.301+0.477 = 0.778 = log 6 ✓.
Trick: Log rules: log(ab)=log a+log b, log(a/b)=log a-log b, log(aⁿ)=n×log a. "Multiply→Add, Divide→Subtract, Power→Multiply."
Q.1496. log(a/b) = Ans: log a - log b
Explanation: Logarithm of a quotient = difference of logarithms. log(8/2) = log 8 - log 2 = 0.903-0.301 = 0.602 = log 4 ✓.
Trick: Division inside log becomes subtraction outside. This is the reverse of the product rule.
Q.1497. log(aⁿ) = Ans: n × log a
Explanation: Power comes down as multiplier. log(2³) = 3×log 2 = 3×0.301 = 0.903 = log 8 ✓.
Trick: This is the most useful log rule for simplification. log(√a) = log(a^½) = ½×log a. log(1/a) = log(a^-1) = -log a.
Q.1498. log 1 = Ans: 0 (for any base)
Explanation: log_b(1) = 0 because b⁰ = 1 for any base b. This is universal — log₂1=0, log₁₀1=0, ln1=0.
Trick: Key log values: log 1=0, log(base)=1 (log₁₀10=1), log 0=undefined (−∞). These are the three anchor points.
Q.1499. Trigonometry: sin 30° = Ans: 1/2; cos 30° = √3/2; tan 30° = 1/√3
Explanation: Standard angles from 30-60-90 triangle (sides 1:√3:2). sin=opposite/hypotenuse=1/2. cos=adjacent/hypotenuse=√3/2. tan=opposite/adjacent=1/√3.
Trick: Memorize table: sin(0,30,45,60,90) = √(0,1,2,3,4)/2 = 0, ½, √2/2, √3/2, 1. cos is reverse. tan=sin/cos.
Q.1500. sin²θ + cos²θ = Ans: 1 (fundamental trigonometric identity)
Explanation: From Pythagoras: in a right triangle with hypotenuse h, opposite o, adjacent a: o²+a²=h². Divide by h²: (o/h)²+(a/h)²=1 → sin²θ+cos²θ=1.
Trick: Three Pythagorean identities: sin²+cos²=1, 1+tan²=sec², 1+cot²=cosec². All derived from the first by dividing by cos² or sin².
Q.1483. If radius of circle is doubled, area becomes: Ans: 4 times (area proportional to r^2)
Q.1484. If radius of sphere is tripled, volume becomes: Ans: 27 times (volume proportional to r^3)
Q.1485. Area of sector with angle theta: Ans: (theta/360) x pi*r^2

10.10 Miscellaneous Quantitative

Q.1486. Allegation rule for mixtures: Ans: (Cheaper qty)/(Dearer qty) = (Dearer price - Mean)/(Mean - Cheaper price)
Q.1487. Clock gains/loses: If a clock gains x minutes per hour, in T hours it gains: Ans: xT minutes
Q.1488. Pipes and Cisterns: If pipe A fills in x hours and B empties in y hours (y>x), net fill rate: Ans: 1/x - 1/y per hour
Q.1489. Calendar: 400 years have exactly: Ans: 0 odd days (400 years = 97 leap + 303 ordinary = 97x2+303x1 = 497 odd days = 71 weeks, 0 odd days)
Q.1490. Races: If A gives B a start of 10m in 100m race, it means: Ans: B starts 10m ahead; A runs 100m while B runs 90m
Q.1491. Geometric Progression sum (infinite, |r|<1): Ans: a/(1-r)
Q.1492. Arithmetic Progression nth term: Ans: a + (n-1)d
Q.1493. AP sum of n terms: Ans: n/2 x [2a + (n-1)d] or n/2 x (first + last)
Q.1494. GP nth term: Ans: a x r^(n-1)
Q.1495. If log(ab) = Ans: log a + log b
Q.1496. If log(a/b) = Ans: log a - log b
Q.1497. log(a^n) = Ans: n x log a
Q.1498. log 1 = Ans: 0 (for any base)
Q.1499. Trigonometry: sin 30 = Ans: 1/2; cos 30 = sqrt(3)/2; tan 30 = 1/sqrt(3)
Q.1500. sin^2(theta) + cos^2(theta) = Ans: 1 (fundamental trigonometric identity)

Section 11: Advanced Indian Polity & Governance (150 Questions)

Topics: Recent Amendments, Tribunals, Anti-Defection Law, RTI, Lokpal, Cooperative Federalism, GST Council, NITI Aayog, Governor's Powers, President's Rule, Inter-State Disputes, CAG, UPSC, Finance Commission, Delimitation

11.1 Recent Constitutional Amendments

Q.1501. The 101st Constitutional Amendment Act deals with: Ans: Goods and Services Tax (GST), introduced in 2017
Q.1502. The 103rd Constitutional Amendment Act provides for: Ans: 10% reservation for Economically Weaker Sections (EWS) in education and government jobs
Q.1503. The 104th Constitutional Amendment Act deals with: Ans: Extension of reservation for SCs and STs in Lok Sabha and State Assemblies till 2030, and removal of reserved seats for Anglo-Indians
Q.1504. The 105th Constitutional Amendment Act restored the power of: Ans: State governments to identify OBCs (Other Backward Classes) for reservation
Q.1505. The 99th Constitutional Amendment Act established: Ans: National Judicial Appointments Commission (NJAC) – later struck down by Supreme Court
Q.1506. Which amendment introduced the right to education as a fundamental right? Ans: 86th Amendment Act, 2002 (Article 21A)
Q.1507. The 97th Constitutional Amendment deals with: Ans: Cooperative Societies – gave constitutional status and protection
Q.1508. The 100th Constitutional Amendment relates to: Ans: Land Boundary Agreement between India and Bangladesh (exchange of enclaves)
Q.1509. Which amendment reduced the voting age from 21 to 18 years? Ans: 61st Amendment Act, 1988
Q.1510. The 73rd and 74th Amendments deal with: Ans: Panchayati Raj Institutions and Urban Local Bodies respectively

11.2 Tribunals & Judicial Bodies

Q.1511. Under which Article are Administrative Tribunals established? Ans: Article 323A (for public service matters)
Q.1512. The National Green Tribunal (NGT) was established in which year? Ans: 2010, under the National Green Tribunal Act
Q.1513. The Central Administrative Tribunal (CAT) was established in: Ans: 1985, under Article 323A
Q.1514. Which tribunal deals with disputes related to armed forces personnel? Ans: Armed Forces Tribunal (AFT), established in 2009
Q.1515. The National Company Law Tribunal (NCLT) was constituted under: Ans: Companies Act, 2013
Q.1516. Article 323B provides for tribunals for: Ans: Tax disputes, industrial disputes, land reforms, food, rent, and other matters specified by Parliament
Q.1517. The Debt Recovery Tribunal (DRT) was established under: Ans: Recovery of Debts Due to Banks and Financial Institutions Act, 1993
Q.1518. The Income Tax Appellate Tribunal (ITAT) is the oldest tribunal in India, established in: Ans: 1941
Q.1519. The Telecom Disputes Settlement and Appellate Tribunal (TDSAT) was established in: Ans: 2000, under TRAI Act
Q.1520. In the case of L. Chandra Kumar vs Union of India (1997), the Supreme Court held that: Ans: Tribunals are subject to judicial review by High Courts under Articles 226/227

11.3 Anti-Defection Law

Q.1521. The Anti-Defection Law was added to the Constitution by which amendment? Ans: 52nd Amendment Act, 1985 (Tenth Schedule)
Q.1522. Under the Tenth Schedule, who decides disqualification on grounds of defection? Ans: The Speaker/Chairman of the respective House
Q.1523. The 91st Amendment Act (2003) made which change to the anti-defection law? Ans: Removed the provision allowing split (1/3rd members) and strengthened the merger provision (2/3rd required)
Q.1524. Under the Tenth Schedule, a merger is valid only if at least what fraction of members agree? Ans: Two-thirds (2/3) of the members of the legislature party
Q.1525. Is the Speaker's decision on defection subject to judicial review? Ans: Yes, as held in Kihoto Hollohan vs Zachillhu (1992), but only on grounds of violation of constitutional mandate
Q.1526. A nominated member can join a political party within what period without attracting disqualification? Ans: Six months from the date of taking seat
Q.1527. The anti-defection law does NOT apply to: Ans: A person elected as Speaker/Chairman (they can rejoin party after ceasing to hold office)
Q.1528. Which committee recommended the anti-defection law? Ans: Y.B. Chavan Committee and Dinesh Goswami Committee on Electoral Reforms
Q.1529. If a member voluntarily gives up membership of a party, it is called: Ans: Defection – leads to disqualification under Tenth Schedule
Q.1530. Can a disqualified member be re-elected? Ans: Yes, a disqualified member can contest fresh elections

11.4 Right to Information (RTI)

Q.1531. The Right to Information Act was enacted in which year? Ans: 2005 (came into force on October 12, 2005)
Q.1532. RTI Act replaced which earlier legislation? Ans: Freedom of Information Act, 2002
Q.1533. Within how many days must a Public Information Officer (PIO) respond to an RTI request? Ans: 30 days (48 hours if matter concerns life or liberty)
Q.1534. The fee for filing an RTI application with Central Government is: Ans: Rs. 10
Q.1535. Which section of RTI Act lists exemptions from disclosure? Ans: Section 8
Q.1536. The Chief Information Commissioner is appointed by: Ans: The President on recommendation of a committee consisting of PM, Leader of Opposition in Lok Sabha, and a Union Cabinet Minister
Q.1537. The term of the Chief Information Commissioner is: Ans: As prescribed by Central Government (RTI Amendment Act, 2019 changed from fixed 5 years)
Q.1538. Which organizations are excluded from the purview of RTI? Ans: Intelligence and security organizations specified in Second Schedule (RAW, IB, etc.) – except for corruption and human rights violations
Q.1539. The first appeal under RTI is made to: Ans: An officer senior in rank to the PIO (First Appellate Authority)
Q.1540. The second appeal under RTI lies with: Ans: Central/State Information Commission

11.5 Lokpal & Lokayukta

Q.1541. The Lokpal and Lokayuktas Act was passed in which year? Ans: 2013
Q.1542. The concept of Lokpal was first recommended by: Ans: First Administrative Reforms Commission (ARC) headed by Morarji Desai (1966)
Q.1543. The first Lokpal of India was: Ans: Justice Pinaki Chandra Ghose (appointed in 2019)
Q.1544. The Lokpal has jurisdiction over: Ans: Prime Minister (with conditions), Union Ministers, MPs, and Group A, B, C, D officers of Central Government
Q.1545. Which state first established the institution of Lokayukta? Ans: Maharashtra (1971)
Q.1546. The Lokpal consists of: Ans: A Chairperson and maximum 8 members (of which 50% shall be judicial members)
Q.1547. The selection committee for Lokpal includes: Ans: PM, Speaker of Lok Sabha, Leader of Opposition in Lok Sabha, CJI or nominee, and an eminent jurist
Q.1548. The Lokpal can initiate inquiry against PM except in matters relating to: Ans: International relations, external and internal security, public order, atomic energy, and space
Q.1549. The term of Lokpal Chairperson and members is: Ans: 5 years or till age of 70, whichever is earlier
Q.1550. The Lokpal can impose penalty up to: Ans: Recommend dismissal and prosecution; can attach property of corrupt public servants

11.6 Cooperative Federalism & GST Council

Q.1551. The GST Council is a constitutional body established under which Article? Ans: Article 279A
Q.1552. Who is the Chairman of the GST Council? Ans: Union Finance Minister
Q.1553. GST was implemented in India from: Ans: July 1, 2017
Q.1554. The GST Council decides by a majority of not less than: Ans: Three-fourths (3/4) of weighted votes cast
Q.1555. In GST Council voting, the Central Government has weightage of: Ans: One-third (1/3) of total votes cast
Q.1556. Which taxes were subsumed under GST? Ans: Central Excise, Service Tax, VAT, CST, Entry Tax, Luxury Tax, Entertainment Tax, Purchase Tax, and others
Q.1557. Which items are kept outside the purview of GST? Ans: Petroleum products (crude oil, petrol, diesel, natural gas, aviation turbine fuel) and alcoholic liquor for human consumption
Q.1558. IGST stands for: Ans: Integrated Goods and Services Tax (levied on inter-state supply)
Q.1559. The Inter-State Council was established under which Article? Ans: Article 263
Q.1560. The Sarkaria Commission (1983) was appointed to examine: Ans: Centre-State relations
Q.1561. The Punchhi Commission (2007) dealt with: Ans: Centre-State relations (successor to Sarkaria Commission)
Q.1562. Cooperative federalism means: Ans: Centre and States cooperate as partners in governance rather than working in isolation
Q.1563. Which body replaced the Planning Commission to promote cooperative federalism? Ans: NITI Aayog (2015)
Q.1564. The Zonal Councils were established under: Ans: States Reorganisation Act, 1956
Q.1565. How many Zonal Councils exist in India? Ans: 5 (Northern, Central, Eastern, Western, Southern) + North-Eastern Council under a separate Act

11.7 NITI Aayog

Q.1566. NITI Aayog was established on: Ans: January 1, 2015
Q.1567. NITI Aayog full form: Ans: National Institution for Transforming India
Q.1568. Who is the Chairperson of NITI Aayog? Ans: Prime Minister of India
Q.1569. The CEO of NITI Aayog is appointed by: Ans: Prime Minister (in the rank of Secretary to Government of India)
Q.1570. NITI Aayog's Governing Council comprises: Ans: All Chief Ministers and Lt. Governors of Union Territories
Q.1571. Unlike Planning Commission, NITI Aayog does NOT have: Ans: Power to allocate funds to ministries/states (it is only an advisory body)
Q.1572. The Aspirational Districts Programme was launched by: Ans: NITI Aayog in January 2018
Q.1573. NITI Aayog publishes which composite index for states? Ans: SDG India Index (Sustainable Development Goals)
Q.1574. The Atal Innovation Mission (AIM) functions under: Ans: NITI Aayog
Q.1575. NITI Aayog replaced the Five Year Plans with: Ans: 15-year Vision Document, 7-year Strategy, and 3-year Action Agenda

11.8 Governor's Powers & President's Rule

Q.1576. The Governor of a State is appointed by: Ans: The President of India (Article 155)
Q.1577. The term of Governor is: Ans: 5 years, but holds office during the pleasure of the President (Article 156)
Q.1578. The Governor's discretionary powers include: Ans: Recommending President's Rule, reserving bills for President's consideration, appointing CM when no clear majority
Q.1579. Article 356 deals with: Ans: President's Rule (failure of constitutional machinery in a state)
Q.1580. President's Rule must be approved by Parliament within: Ans: 2 months
Q.1581. Maximum duration of President's Rule is: Ans: 3 years (with Parliamentary approval every 6 months)
Q.1582. In S.R. Bommai vs Union of India (1994), the Supreme Court held that: Ans: President's Rule is subject to judicial review; floor test is the proper way to determine majority
Q.1583. Article 365 states that President's Rule can be imposed if: Ans: A state fails to comply with directions of the Union
Q.1584. During President's Rule, the state legislature is: Ans: Suspended or dissolved; Parliament exercises legislative power for the state
Q.1585. The Governor can reserve a bill for President's consideration under: Ans: Article 200
Q.1586. The Governor has the power to grant pardons under: Ans: Article 161 (for offences against state laws)
Q.1587. Can the Governor pardon a death sentence? Ans: No, only the President can pardon a death sentence (Article 72)
Q.1588. The Governor's address to the state legislature is under: Ans: Article 175
Q.1589. The Governor can promulgate ordinances under: Ans: Article 213 (when state legislature is not in session)
Q.1590. The first state where President's Rule was imposed: Ans: Punjab (1951) – under PEPSU (Patiala and East Punjab States Union)

11.9 Inter-State Relations & Disputes

Q.1591. Inter-state water disputes are adjudicated under: Ans: Article 262 and Inter-State Water Disputes Act, 1956
Q.1592. The Inter-State Water Disputes Tribunal's decision is: Ans: Final and binding; cannot be questioned in any court (Article 262(2))
Q.1593. The Krishna Water Disputes Tribunal was constituted in: Ans: 1969 (Bachawat Tribunal); reconstituted as KWDT-II in 2004 (Brijesh Kumar Tribunal)
Q.1594. The Godavari Water Disputes Tribunal award was given in: Ans: 1980
Q.1595. Article 263 provides for: Ans: Establishment of Inter-State Council by the President
Q.1596. Freedom of inter-state trade and commerce is guaranteed under: Ans: Article 301
Q.1597. Article 307 empowers Parliament to appoint: Ans: An authority for carrying out purposes of Articles 301-304 (inter-state trade)
Q.1598. Disputes between states are adjudicated by: Ans: Supreme Court under its original jurisdiction (Article 131)
Q.1599. The River Boards Act was enacted in: Ans: 1956 (for regulation and development of inter-state rivers)
Q.1600. Full faith and credit to public acts and judicial proceedings of states is given under: Ans: Article 261

11.10 CAG, UPSC & Finance Commission

Q.1601. The Comptroller and Auditor General (CAG) is appointed under: Ans: Article 148
Q.1602. CAG can be removed by: Ans: Same procedure as removal of a Supreme Court Judge (impeachment)
Q.1603. CAG submits audit reports to: Ans: The President (for Union) or Governor (for State), who places them before Parliament/Legislature
Q.1604. The Public Accounts Committee (PAC) examines: Ans: The audit reports of CAG
Q.1605. Dr. B.R. Ambedkar called CAG as: Ans: The most important officer under the Constitution of India
Q.1606. UPSC is constituted under: Ans: Article 315
Q.1607. Members of UPSC are appointed by: Ans: The President of India
Q.1608. A member of UPSC can be removed by: Ans: The President on the report of the Supreme Court (Article 317)
Q.1609. The Finance Commission is constituted under: Ans: Article 280
Q.1610. The Finance Commission is constituted every: Ans: 5 years (or earlier as the President considers necessary)
Q.1611. The 15th Finance Commission was chaired by: Ans: N.K. Singh (for the period 2021-26)
Q.1612. The Finance Commission recommends: Ans: Distribution of net proceeds of taxes between Centre and States, grants-in-aid, and measures to augment state finances
Q.1613. The 15th Finance Commission recommended what share of central taxes to states? Ans: 41% of the divisible pool
Q.1614. The Election Commission is constituted under: Ans: Article 324
Q.1615. The Chief Election Commissioner can be removed by: Ans: Same manner as a Supreme Court Judge (impeachment by Parliament)

11.11 Delimitation & Electoral Reforms

Q.1616. Delimitation means: Ans: The process of fixing boundaries of territorial constituencies for elections
Q.1617. The Delimitation Commission is appointed by: Ans: The President of India under the Delimitation Commission Act
Q.1618. How many Delimitation Commissions have been constituted so far? Ans: 4 (in 1952, 1963, 1973, and 2002)
Q.1619. The 84th Amendment (2002) froze delimitation of constituencies until: Ans: 2026 (based on 2001 census for readjustment of seats)
Q.1620. EVM (Electronic Voting Machine) was first used in India in: Ans: 1982 (Kerala Assembly by-election in Parur)
Q.1621. VVPAT stands for: Ans: Voter Verifiable Paper Audit Trail
Q.1622. NOTA option was introduced in Indian elections from: Ans: 2013 (based on Supreme Court directive in PUCL vs Union of India)
Q.1623. The Model Code of Conduct comes into force: Ans: From the date of announcement of elections by the Election Commission
Q.1624. Representation of People Act, 1951 deals with: Ans: Conduct of elections, qualifications and disqualifications, corrupt practices, election disputes
Q.1625. The maximum election expenditure limit for Assembly elections (as revised) is: Ans: Rs. 40 lakh (revised in 2022; varies by state)

11.12 Miscellaneous Polity & Governance

Q.1626. The National Human Rights Commission (NHRC) was established in: Ans: 1993, under the Protection of Human Rights Act, 1993
Q.1627. The Chairperson of NHRC is: Ans: A retired Chief Justice of India
Q.1628. The National Commission for Women was established in: Ans: 1992, under the National Commission for Women Act, 1990
Q.1629. The National Commission for SCs is established under: Ans: Article 338
Q.1630. The National Commission for STs is established under: Ans: Article 338A (inserted by 89th Amendment, 2003)
Q.1631. The National Commission for Backward Classes is under: Ans: Article 338B (inserted by 102nd Amendment, 2018)
Q.1632. The Official Language of the Union is: Ans: Hindi in Devanagari script (Article 343)
Q.1633. The Eighth Schedule of the Constitution lists: Ans: 22 recognized languages
Q.1634. Which languages were added by the 92nd Amendment (2003) to the Eighth Schedule? Ans: Bodo, Dogri, Maithili, and Santhali
Q.1635. The concept of 'Public Interest Litigation' (PIL) was introduced by: Ans: Justice P.N. Bhagwati and Justice V.R. Krishna Iyer
Q.1636. Article 32 provides for: Ans: Right to Constitutional Remedies (right to move Supreme Court for enforcement of Fundamental Rights)
Q.1637. The five writs that can be issued by Supreme Court and High Courts are: Ans: Habeas Corpus, Mandamus, Prohibition, Certiorari, Quo Warranto
Q.1638. Habeas Corpus means: Ans: "To have the body" – issued against unlawful detention
Q.1639. Mandamus means: Ans: "We command" – issued to a public authority to perform its duty
Q.1640. Quo Warranto means: Ans: "By what authority" – issued to inquire into legality of a person holding public office
Q.1641. The Panchayati Raj system in India is based on the recommendation of: Ans: Balwant Rai Mehta Committee (1957)
Q.1642. The 73rd Amendment mandates reservation for women in Panchayats of: Ans: Not less than one-third (33%) of total seats
Q.1643. The State Finance Commission for Panchayats is constituted under: Ans: Article 243-I
Q.1644. District Planning Committee is constituted under: Ans: Article 243ZD
Q.1645. The 74th Amendment deals with: Ans: Urban Local Bodies (Municipalities, Municipal Corporations, Nagar Panchayats)
Q.1646. The Twelfth Schedule lists functions of: Ans: Municipalities (18 subjects)
Q.1647. The Eleventh Schedule lists functions of: Ans: Panchayats (29 subjects)
Q.1648. Article 368 deals with: Ans: Power of Parliament to amend the Constitution
Q.1649. A Constitutional Amendment Bill can be introduced in: Ans: Either House of Parliament (no prior permission of President needed)
Q.1650. For amending federal provisions, ratification by how many state legislatures is needed? Ans: Not less than one-half (50%) of state legislatures

Section 12: Modern Indian History & Freedom Struggle (150 Questions)

Topics: Revolt of 1857, Indian National Congress Sessions, Moderate vs Extremist Era, Gandhi's Movements, Subhas Chandra Bose, Revolutionary Movements, Communal Award, Cabinet Mission, Partition, Social Reform Movements, Press Acts

12.1 Revolt of 1857 & Early Resistance

Q.1651. The Revolt of 1857 started on: Ans: May 10, 1857 at Meerut
Q.1652. The immediate cause of the Revolt of 1857 was: Ans: Introduction of Enfield rifles with greased cartridges (rumored to contain cow and pig fat)
Q.1653. The first Indian soldier to refuse the greased cartridge was: Ans: Mangal Pandey (at Barrackpore, March 29, 1857)
Q.1654. The rebels declared whom as the Emperor of India during 1857 revolt? Ans: Bahadur Shah Zafar II (last Mughal Emperor)
Q.1655. The leader of revolt at Kanpur was: Ans: Nana Sahib (with Tantia Tope as military commander)
Q.1656. Rani Lakshmibai led the revolt at: Ans: Jhansi
Q.1657. The revolt at Lucknow was led by: Ans: Begum Hazrat Mahal
Q.1658. Kunwar Singh led the revolt in: Ans: Bihar (Jagdishpur)
Q.1659. After the Revolt of 1857, the British Crown took over administration from: Ans: East India Company (through Government of India Act, 1858)
Q.1660. Queen Victoria's Proclamation (1858) promised: Ans: Non-interference in religious matters, equal treatment, and amnesty to rebels (except those who killed British)
Q.1661. V.D. Savarkar called the Revolt of 1857 as: Ans: "The First War of Indian Independence"
Q.1662. The Doctrine of Lapse was introduced by: Ans: Lord Dalhousie
Q.1663. States annexed under Doctrine of Lapse include: Ans: Satara, Jhansi, Nagpur, Jaitpur, Sambhalpur, Udaipur
Q.1664. The Subsidiary Alliance system was introduced by: Ans: Lord Wellesley
Q.1665. The Permanent Settlement (1793) was introduced by: Ans: Lord Cornwallis in Bengal, Bihar, and Orissa

12.2 Indian National Congress & Moderate Era

Q.1666. The Indian National Congress was founded in: Ans: 1885 at Bombay (Gokuldas Tejpal Sanskrit College)
Q.1667. The founder of Indian National Congress was: Ans: A.O. Hume (Allan Octavian Hume)
Q.1668. The first President of INC was: Ans: W.C. Bonnerjee (Womesh Chunder Bonnerjee)
Q.1669. The first session of INC was attended by: Ans: 72 delegates
Q.1670. The 'Safety Valve' theory about INC was proposed by: Ans: Lala Lajpat Rai (suggesting Congress was created to prevent revolution)
Q.1671. The Moderate leaders of INC included: Ans: Dadabhai Naoroji, Gopal Krishna Gokhale, Surendranath Banerjee, Pherozeshah Mehta
Q.1672. Dadabhai Naoroji's 'Drain Theory' was presented in his book: Ans: "Poverty and Un-British Rule in India" (1901)
Q.1673. The methods of Moderates included: Ans: Prayers, petitions, constitutional agitation, and appeals to British sense of justice
Q.1674. Dadabhai Naoroji was the first Indian to be elected to: Ans: British House of Commons (1892, from Finsbury Central)
Q.1675. The INC session that first demanded 'Swaraj' was: Ans: Calcutta Session, 1906 (presided by Dadabhai Naoroji)

12.3 Extremist Era & Partition of Bengal

Q.1676. The Partition of Bengal was announced by: Ans: Lord Curzon in 1905 (effective October 16, 1905)
Q.1677. The Swadeshi Movement was launched in response to: Ans: Partition of Bengal (1905)
Q.1678. The trio of Extremist leaders (Lal-Bal-Pal) were: Ans: Lala Lajpat Rai, Bal Gangadhar Tilak, Bipin Chandra Pal
Q.1679. Tilak's famous declaration was: Ans: "Swaraj is my birthright and I shall have it"
Q.1680. The Surat Split (1907) was between: Ans: Moderates and Extremists in INC
Q.1681. The Partition of Bengal was annulled in: Ans: 1911 (by Lord Hardinge)
Q.1682. The Morley-Minto Reforms (1909) introduced: Ans: Separate electorate for Muslims (Indian Councils Act, 1909)
Q.1683. The Muslim League was founded in: Ans: 1906 at Dhaka (under Aga Khan III)
Q.1684. The Lucknow Pact (1916) was between: Ans: INC and Muslim League (Congress accepted separate electorates)
Q.1685. The Home Rule Movement was started by: Ans: Tilak (April 1916, in Maharashtra) and Annie Besant (September 1916, rest of India)

12.4 Gandhian Era – Early Movements

Q.1686. Mahatma Gandhi returned to India from South Africa in: Ans: January 1915
Q.1687. Gandhi's first Satyagraha in India was at: Ans: Champaran (Bihar) in 1917 (against indigo planters)
Q.1688. The Kheda Satyagraha (1918) was for: Ans: Remission of land revenue during famine in Gujarat
Q.1689. The Ahmedabad Mill Strike (1918) was Gandhi's first: Ans: Hunger strike in India (for textile workers' wages)
Q.1690. The Rowlatt Act (1919) provided for: Ans: Detention without trial and trial without jury (called "Black Act" by Indians)
Q.1691. The Jallianwala Bagh Massacre occurred on: Ans: April 13, 1919 at Amritsar (ordered by General Dyer)
Q.1692. The Hunter Commission was appointed to investigate: Ans: Jallianwala Bagh Massacre
Q.1693. Who returned his knighthood in protest against Jallianwala Bagh? Ans: Rabindranath Tagore
Q.1694. The Khilafat Movement (1919-1924) was launched for: Ans: Restoration of the Caliphate in Turkey (led by Ali Brothers – Mohammad Ali and Shaukat Ali)
Q.1695. The Non-Cooperation Movement was launched in: Ans: 1920 (approved at Calcutta special session of INC)
Q.1696. Gandhi called off Non-Cooperation Movement after: Ans: Chauri Chaura incident (February 5, 1922) where a mob burned a police station killing 22 policemen
Q.1697. The Swaraj Party was formed in 1923 by: Ans: C.R. Das and Motilal Nehru (to contest elections and obstruct from within)
Q.1698. The Simon Commission visited India in: Ans: 1928 (boycotted because it had no Indian member)
Q.1699. "Simon Go Back" slogan was raised during protests against: Ans: Simon Commission; Lala Lajpat Rai was fatally injured in lathi charge at Lahore
Q.1700. The Nehru Report (1928) demanded: Ans: Dominion Status for India (drafted by Motilal Nehru)

12.5 Civil Disobedience & Round Table Conferences

Q.1701. The Purna Swaraj (Complete Independence) resolution was passed at: Ans: Lahore Session of INC, 1929 (presided by Jawaharlal Nehru)
Q.1702. The first Independence Day was celebrated on: Ans: January 26, 1930
Q.1703. The Salt March (Dandi March) began on: Ans: March 12, 1930 from Sabarmati Ashram to Dandi (241 miles, 24 days)
Q.1704. Gandhi broke the salt law at Dandi on: Ans: April 6, 1930
Q.1705. The First Round Table Conference (1930) was boycotted by: Ans: Indian National Congress
Q.1706. The Gandhi-Irwin Pact was signed on: Ans: March 5, 1931 (Congress agreed to attend Second RTC)
Q.1707. Gandhi attended which Round Table Conference? Ans: Second Round Table Conference (1931) as sole Congress representative
Q.1708. The Communal Award (1932) was announced by: Ans: British PM Ramsay MacDonald (provided separate electorates for depressed classes)
Q.1709. The Poona Pact (1932) was between: Ans: Gandhi and Dr. B.R. Ambedkar (replaced separate electorates with reserved seats for depressed classes within joint electorate)
Q.1710. The Government of India Act, 1935 provided for: Ans: Provincial autonomy, All-India Federation (never implemented), Federal Court, and bicameral legislatures in some provinces
Q.1711. In the 1937 elections, Congress won majority in how many provinces? Ans: 7 out of 11 provinces
Q.1712. Congress ministries resigned in 1939 because: Ans: India was declared a party to WWII without consulting Indian leaders
Q.1713. The Muslim League celebrated 'Deliverance Day' on: Ans: December 22, 1939 (after Congress ministries resigned)
Q.1714. The Pakistan Resolution (Lahore Resolution) was passed on: Ans: March 23, 1940 (at Muslim League session in Lahore)
Q.1715. The Individual Satyagraha (1940-41) was started by: Ans: Gandhi; Vinoba Bhave was the first Satyagrahi

12.6 Quit India Movement & INA

Q.1716. The Cripps Mission (1942) was sent to India to: Ans: Secure Indian cooperation in WWII; offered Dominion Status after war (rejected by Congress)
Q.1717. Gandhi described the Cripps proposals as: Ans: "A post-dated cheque on a crashing bank"
Q.1718. The Quit India Movement was launched on: Ans: August 8, 1942 (at Bombay session of AICC)
Q.1719. Gandhi's call during Quit India Movement was: Ans: "Do or Die" (Karo ya Maro)
Q.1720. During Quit India Movement, parallel governments were formed at: Ans: Ballia (UP), Tamluk (Bengal), Satara (Maharashtra)
Q.1721. The Indian National Army (INA) was first organized by: Ans: Captain Mohan Singh (1942) with Japanese support
Q.1722. Subhas Chandra Bose reorganized INA and gave the slogan: Ans: "Give me blood, I will give you freedom" and "Jai Hind"
Q.1723. The Azad Hind Government was formed by Bose in: Ans: October 21, 1943 at Singapore
Q.1724. The INA trials were held at: Ans: Red Fort, Delhi (1945) – defended by Bhulabhai Desai, Tej Bahadur Sapru, and Jawaharlal Nehru
Q.1725. Subhas Chandra Bose escaped from India in 1941 and reached: Ans: Germany (via Afghanistan), later went to Japan and Southeast Asia
Q.1726. The Forward Bloc was founded by: Ans: Subhas Chandra Bose in 1939
Q.1727. Bose was elected INC President at which session? Ans: Haripura (1938) and Tripuri (1939) – resigned after Tripuri due to conflict with Gandhi
Q.1728. The Royal Indian Navy Mutiny occurred in: Ans: February 1946 at Bombay
Q.1729. The Wavell Plan and Shimla Conference was held in: Ans: June 1945 (failed due to Jinnah's insistence on Muslim League nominating all Muslim members)
Q.1730. The Cabinet Mission (1946) proposed: Ans: A three-tier federal structure; rejected the demand for Pakistan; proposed a Constituent Assembly

12.7 Towards Independence & Partition

Q.1731. The Cabinet Mission consisted of: Ans: Lord Pethick-Lawrence, Sir Stafford Cripps, and A.V. Alexander
Q.1732. The Direct Action Day was called by Muslim League on: Ans: August 16, 1946 (led to Great Calcutta Killings)
Q.1733. The Interim Government was formed on: Ans: September 2, 1946 (headed by Jawaharlal Nehru)
Q.1734. The Mountbatten Plan (June 3, 1947) announced: Ans: Partition of India into India and Pakistan
Q.1735. The Indian Independence Act was passed by British Parliament on: Ans: July 18, 1947 (received Royal Assent)
Q.1736. The Radcliffe Line demarcated: Ans: The boundary between India and Pakistan (drawn by Sir Cyril Radcliffe)
Q.1737. India became independent on: Ans: August 15, 1947 (midnight of August 14-15)
Q.1738. The first Governor-General of independent India was: Ans: Lord Mountbatten
Q.1739. The last Governor-General of India was: Ans: C. Rajagopalachari (1948-1950)
Q.1740. The Constituent Assembly was formed based on: Ans: Cabinet Mission Plan (1946)
Q.1741. The Constituent Assembly had how many members? Ans: 389 initially; reduced to 299 after partition
Q.1742. The Drafting Committee of the Constitution was headed by: Ans: Dr. B.R. Ambedkar
Q.1743. The Constitution was adopted on: Ans: November 26, 1949 (celebrated as Constitution Day)
Q.1744. The integration of princely states was achieved by: Ans: Sardar Vallabhbhai Patel (with V.P. Menon as secretary)
Q.1745. Operation Polo (1948) was the military action to integrate: Ans: Hyderabad State into Indian Union

12.8 Revolutionary Movements

Q.1746. The Hindustan Republican Association (HRA) was founded in: Ans: 1924 by Sachindranath Sanyal, Ram Prasad Bismil, and others
